But I make sacrifices too, sometimes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you call sacrifices?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, spending the winter at Sorrento.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard began to laugh, and then he told her she must have had a very\r\nhappy life--\xe2\x80\x9cto call a winter at Sorrento a sacrifice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt depends upon what one gives up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Vivian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you give up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe touched him with her mocking smile again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is not a very civil question, asked in that way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean that I seem to doubt your abnegation?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou seem to insinuate that I had nothing to renounce. I gave up--I\r\ngave up--\xe2\x80\x9d and she looked about her, considering a little--\xe2\x80\x9cI gave up\r\nsociety.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad you remember what it was,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I have seemed\r\nuncivil, let me make it up. When a woman speaks of giving up society,\r\nwhat she means is giving up admiration. You can never have given up\r\nthat--you can never have escaped from it. You must have found it even at\r\nSorrento.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt may have been there, but I never found it. It was very\r\nrespectful--it never expressed itself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is the deepest kind,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI prefer the shallower varieties,\xe2\x80\x9d the young girl answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, \xe2\x80\x9cyou must remember that although shallow\r\nadmiration expresses itself, all the admiration that expresses itself is\r\nnot shallow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Vivian hesitated a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome of it is impertinent,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, looking straight at him, rather\r\ngravely.\r\n\r\nBernard hesitated about as long.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen it is impertinent it is shallow. That comes to the same thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young girl frowned a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not sure that I understand--I am rather stupid. But you see how\r\nright I am in my taste for such places as this. I have to come here to\r\nhear such ingenious remarks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should add that my coming, as well, has something to do with it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything!\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Vivian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything? Does no one else make ingenious remarks? Does n\xe2\x80\x99t my friend\r\nWright?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Wright says excellent things, but I should not exactly call them\r\ningenious remarks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not what Wright says; it \xe2\x80\x98s what he does. That \xe2\x80\x98s the charm!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Bernard.\r\n\r\nHis companion was silent for a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cThat \xe2\x80\x98s not usually a charm;\r\ngood conduct is not thought pleasing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt surely is not thought the reverse!\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt does n\xe2\x80\x99t rank--in the opinion of most people--among the things that\r\nmake men agreeable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt depends upon what you call agreeable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly so,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Vivian. \xe2\x80\x9cIt all depends on that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the agreeable,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard went on--\xe2\x80\x9cit is n\xe2\x80\x99t after all, fortunately,\r\nsuch a subtle idea! The world certainly is agreed to think that virtue\r\nis a beautiful thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Vivian dropped her eyes a moment, and then, looking up,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it a charm?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor me there is no charm without it,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard declared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid that for me there is,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young girl.\r\n\r\nBernard was puzzled--he who was not often puzzled. His companion\r\nstruck him as altogether too clever to be likely to indulge in a silly\r\naffectation of cynicism. And yet, without this, how could one account\r\nfor her sneering at virtue?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou talk as if you had sounded the depths of vice!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, laughing.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you know about other than virtuous charms?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know, of course, nothing about vice; but I have known virtue when it\r\nwas very tiresome.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, then it was a poor affair. It was poor virtue. The best virtue is\r\nnever tiresome.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Vivian looked at him a little, with her fine discriminating eye.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a dreadful thing to have to think any virtue poor!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was a touching reflection, and it might have gone further had not\r\nthe conversation been interrupted by Mrs. Vivian\xe2\x80\x99s appealing to her\r\ndaughter to aid a defective recollection of a story about a Spanish\r\nfamily they had met at Biarritz, with which she had undertaken to\r\nentertain Gordon Wright. After this, the little circle was joined by\r\na party of American friends who were spending a week at Baden, and the\r\nconversation became general.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII\r\n\r\nBut on the following evening, Bernard again found himself seated in\r\nfriendly colloquy with this interesting girl, while Gordon Wright\r\ndiscoursed with her mother on one side, and little Blanche Evers\r\nchattered to the admiring eyes of Captain Lovelock on the other.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou and your mother are very kind to that little girl,\xe2\x80\x9d our hero said;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cyou must be a great advantage to her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAngela Vivian directed her eyes to her neighbors, and let them rest\r\na while on the young girl\xe2\x80\x99s little fidgeting figure and her fresh,\r\ncoquettish face. For some moments she said nothing, and to Longueville,\r\nturning over several things in his mind, and watching her, it seemed\r\nthat her glance was one of disfavor. He divined, he scarcely knew how,\r\nthat her esteem for her pretty companion was small.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I am very kind,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Vivian. \xe2\x80\x9cI have done\r\nnothing in particular for her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Wright tells me you came to this place mainly on her account.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI came for myself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Vivian. \xe2\x80\x9cThe consideration you speak of\r\nperhaps had weight with my mother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not an easy person to say appreciative things to,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard\r\nrejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cOne is tempted to say them; but you don\xe2\x80\x99t take them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young girl colored as she listened to this observation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think you know,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured, looking away. Then, \xe2\x80\x9cSet it down\r\nto modesty,\xe2\x80\x9d she added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat, of course, is what I have done. To what else could one possibly\r\nattribute an indifference to compliments?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is something else. One might be proud.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere you are again!\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cYou won\xe2\x80\x99t even let me praise\r\nyour modesty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would rather you should rebuke my pride.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is so humble a speech that it leaves no room for rebuke.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor a moment Miss Vivian said nothing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMen are singularly base,\xe2\x80\x9d she declared presently, with a little smile.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey don\xe2\x80\x99t care in the least to say things that might help a person.\r\nThey only care to say things that may seem effective and agreeable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see: you think that to say agreeable things is a great misdemeanor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt comes from their vanity,\xe2\x80\x9d Miss Vivian went on, as if she had not\r\nheard him. \xe2\x80\x9cThey wish to appear agreeable and get credit for cleverness\r\nand tendresse, no matter how silly it would be for another person to\r\nbelieve them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard was a good deal amused, and a little nettled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWomen, then,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9chave rather a fondness for producing a bad\r\nimpression--they like to appear disagreeable?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis companion bent her eyes upon her fan for a moment as she opened and\r\nclosed it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are capable of resigning themselves to it--for a purpose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard was moved to extreme merriment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor what purpose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I mean for a purpose,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Vivian; \xe2\x80\x9cbut for a\r\nnecessity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, what an odious necessity!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNecessities usually are odious. But women meet them. Men evade them and\r\nshirk them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI contest your proposition. Women are themselves necessities; but they\r\nare not odious ones!\xe2\x80\x9d And Bernard added, in a moment, \xe2\x80\x9cOne could n\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nevade them, if they were!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI object to being called a necessity,\xe2\x80\x9d said Angela Vivian. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\ndiminishes one\xe2\x80\x99s merit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but it enhances the charm of life!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor men, doubtless!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe charm of life is very great,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard went on, looking up at the\r\ndusky hills and the summer stars, seen through a sort of mist of music\r\nand talk, and of powdery light projected from the softly lurid windows\r\nof the gaming-rooms. \xe2\x80\x9cThe charm of life is extreme. I am unacquainted\r\nwith odious necessities. I object to nothing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAngela Vivian looked about her as he had done--looked perhaps a moment\r\nlonger at the summer stars; and if she had not already proved herself a\r\nyoung lady of a contradictory turn, it might have been supposed she was\r\njust then tacitly admitting the charm of life to be considerable.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you suppose Miss Evers often resigns herself to being\r\ndisagreeable--for a purpose?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Longueville, who had glanced at\r\nCaptain Lovelock\xe2\x80\x99s companion again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe can\xe2\x80\x99t be disagreeable; she is too gentle, too soft.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean too silly?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I call her silly. She is not very wise; but she has\r\nno pretensions--absolutely none--so that one is not struck with anything\r\nincongruous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a terrible description! I suppose one ought to have a few\r\npretensions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see one comes off more easily without them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Vivian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you call that coming off easily?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked at him a moment gravely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very fond of Blanche,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCaptain Lovelock is rather fond of her,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard went on.\r\n\r\nThe girl assented.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is completely fascinated--he is very much in love with her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd do they mean to make an international match?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope not; my mother and I are greatly troubled.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs n\xe2\x80\x99t he a good fellow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is a good fellow; but he is a mere trifler. He has n\xe2\x80\x99t a penny, I\r\nbelieve, and he has very expensive habits. He gambles a great deal. We\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know what to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should send for the young lady\xe2\x80\x99s mother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have written to her pressingly. She answers that Blanche can take\r\ncare of herself, and that she must stay at Marienbad to finish her cure.\r\nShe has just begun a new one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, \xe2\x80\x9cdoubtless Blanche can take care of herself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor a moment his companion said nothing; then she exclaimed--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt \xe2\x80\x98s what a girl ought to be able to do!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure you are!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard.\r\n\r\nShe met his eyes, and she was going to make some rejoinder; but before\r\nshe had time to speak, her mother\xe2\x80\x99s little, clear, conciliatory voice\r\ninterposed. Mrs. Vivian appealed to her daughter, as she had done the\r\nnight before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear Angela, what was the name of the gentleman who delivered that\r\ndelightful course of lectures that we heard in Geneva, on--what was the\r\ntitle?--\xe2\x80\x98The Redeeming Features of the Pagan Morality.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAngela flushed a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have quite forgotten his name, mamma,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, without looking\r\nround.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome and sit by me, my dear, and we will talk them over. I wish Mr.\r\nWright to hear about them,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Vivian went on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you wish to convert him to paganism?\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe lectures were very dull; they had no redeeming features,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nAngela, getting up, but turning away from her mother. She stood\r\nlooking at Bernard Longueville; he saw she was annoyed at her mother\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ninterference. \xe2\x80\x9cEvery now and then,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI take a turn through\r\nthe gaming-rooms. The last time, Captain Lovelock went with me. Will you\r\ncome to-night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard assented with expressive alacrity; he was charmed with her not\r\nwishing to break off her conversation with him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, we \xe2\x80\x98ll all go!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Vivian, who had been listening, and she\r\ninvited the others to accompany her to the Kursaal.\r\n\r\nThey left their places, but Angela went first, with Bernard Longueville\r\nby her side; and the idea of her having publicly braved her mother,\r\nas it were, for the sake of his society, lent for the moment an almost\r\necstatic energy to his tread. If he had been tempted to presume upon his\r\ntriumph, however, he would have found a check in the fact that the young\r\ngirl herself tasted very soberly of the sweets of defiance. She\r\nwas silent and grave; she had a manner which took the edge from the\r\nwantonness of filial independence. Yet, for all this, Bernard was\r\npleased with his position; and, as he walked with her through the\r\nlighted and crowded rooms, where they soon detached themselves from\r\ntheir companions, he felt that peculiar satisfaction which best\r\nexpresses itself in silence. Angela looked a while at the rows of still,\r\nattentive faces, fixed upon the luminous green circle, across which\r\nlittle heaps of louis d\xe2\x80\x99or were being pushed to and fro, and she\r\ncontinued to say nothing. Then at last she exclaimed simply, \xe2\x80\x9cCome\r\naway!\xe2\x80\x9d They turned away and passed into another chamber, in which there\r\nwas no gambling. It was an immense apartment, apparently a ball-room;\r\nbut at present it was quite unoccupied. There were green velvet benches\r\nall around it, and a great polished floor stretched away, shining in the\r\nlight of chandeliers adorned with innumerable glass drops. Miss Vivian\r\nstood a moment on the threshold; then she passed in, and they stopped\r\nin the middle of the place, facing each other, and with their figures\r\nreflected as if they had been standing on a sheet of ice. There was no\r\none in the room; they were entirely alone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you recognize me?\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard murmured quickly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRecognize you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you seem to forget our meeting at Siena?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe might have answered if she had answered immediately; but she\r\nhesitated, and while she did so something happened at the other end of\r\nthe room which caused her to shift her glance. A green velvet portiere\r\nsuspended in one of the door-ways--not that through which our\r\nfriends had passed--was lifted, and Gordon Wright stood there, holding\r\nit up, and looking at them. His companions were behind him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, here they are!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Gordon, in his loud, clear voice.\r\n\r\nThis appeared to strike Angela Vivian as an interruption, and Bernard\r\nsaw it very much in the same light.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII\r\n\r\nHe forbore to ask her his question again--she might tell him at her\r\nconvenience. But the days passed by, and she never told him--she had\r\nher own reasons. Bernard talked with her very often; conversation formed\r\nindeed the chief entertainment of the quiet little circle of which\r\nhe was a member. They sat on the terrace and talked in the mingled\r\nstarlight and lamplight, and they strolled in the deep green forests and\r\nwound along the side of the gentle Baden hills, under the influence of\r\ncolloquial tendencies. The Black Forest is a country of almost unbroken\r\nshade, and in the still days of midsummer the whole place was covered\r\nwith a motionless canopy of verdure. Our friends were not extravagant\r\nor audacious people, and they looked at Baden life very much from the\r\noutside--they sat aloof from the brightly lighted drama of professional\r\nrevelry. Among themselves as well, however, a little drama went\r\nforward in which each member of the company had a part to play. Bernard\r\nLongueville had been surprised at first at what he would have called\r\nMiss Vivian\xe2\x80\x99s approachableness--at the frequency with which he\r\nencountered opportunities for sitting near her and entering into\r\nconversation. He had expected that Gordon Wright would deem himself to\r\nhave established an anticipatory claim upon the young lady\xe2\x80\x99s attention,\r\nand that, in pursuance of this claim, he would occupy a recognized place\r\nat her side. Gordon was, after all, wooing her; it was very natural\r\nhe should seek her society. In fact, he was never very far off; but\r\nBernard, for three or four days, had the anomalous consciousness of\r\nbeing still nearer. Presently, however, he perceived that he owed this\r\nprivilege simply to his friend\xe2\x80\x99s desire that he should become acquainted\r\nwith Miss Vivian--should receive a vivid impression of a person in\r\nwhom Gordon was so deeply interested. After this result might have been\r\nsupposed to be attained, Gordon Wright stepped back into his usual place\r\nand showed her those small civilities which were the only homage that\r\nthe quiet conditions of their life rendered possible--walked with her,\r\ntalked with her, brought her a book to read, a chair to sit upon, a\r\ncouple of flowers to place in the bosom of her gown, treated her, in a\r\nword, with a sober but by no means inexpressive gallantry. He had\r\nnot been making violent love, as he told Longueville, and these\r\ndemonstrations were certainly not violent. Bernard said to himself\r\nthat if he were not in the secret, a spectator would scarcely make the\r\ndiscovery that Gordon cherished an even very safely tended flame. Angela\r\nVivian, on her side, was not strikingly responsive. There was nothing\r\nin her deportment to indicate that she was in love with her systematic\r\nsuitor. She was perfectly gracious and civil. She smiled in his face\r\nwhen he shook hands with her; she looked at him and listened when he\r\ntalked; she let him stroll beside her in the Lichtenthal Alley; she\r\nread, or appeared to read, the books he lent her, and she decorated\r\nherself with the flowers he offered. She seemed neither bored nor\r\nembarrassed, neither irritated nor oppressed. But it was Bernard\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbelief that she took no more pleasure in his attentions than a pretty\r\ngirl must always take in any recognition of her charms. \xe2\x80\x9cIf she \xe2\x80\x98s\r\nnot indifferent,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cshe is, at any rate,\r\nimpartial--profoundly impartial.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was not till the end of a week that Gordon Wright told him exactly\r\nhow his business stood with Miss Vivian and what he had reason to expect\r\nand hope--a week during which their relations had been of the happiest\r\nand most comfortable cast, and during which Bernard, rejoicing in\r\ntheir long walks and talks, in the charming weather, in the beauty and\r\nentertainment of the place, and in other things besides, had not ceased\r\nto congratulate himself on coming to Baden. Bernard, after the first\r\nday, had asked his friend no questions. He had a great respect for\r\nopportunity, coming either to others or to himself, and he left Gordon\r\nto turn his lantern as fitfully as might be upon the subject which was\r\ntacitly open between them, but of which as yet only the mere edges had\r\nemerged into light. Gordon, on his side, seemed content for the moment\r\nwith having his clever friend under his hand; he reserved him for final\r\nappeal or for some other mysterious use.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t tell me you don\xe2\x80\x99t know her now,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, one evening as the\r\ntwo young men strolled along the Lichtenthal Alley--\xe2\x80\x9cnow that you have\r\nhad a whole week\xe2\x80\x99s observation of her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is a week\xe2\x80\x99s observation of a singularly clever and complicated\r\nwoman?\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, your week has been of some use. You have found out she is\r\ncomplicated!\xe2\x80\x9d Gordon rejoined.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Gordon,\xe2\x80\x9d Longueville exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see what it signifies\r\nto you that I should find Miss Vivian out! When a man \xe2\x80\x98s in love, what\r\nneed he care what other people think of the loved object?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would certainly be a pity to care too much. But there is some excuse\r\nfor him in the loved object being, as you say, complicated.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense! That \xe2\x80\x98s no excuse. The loved object is always complicated.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGordon walked on in silence a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, I don\xe2\x80\x99t care a button what you think!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBravo! That \xe2\x80\x98s the way a man should talk,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Longueville.\r\n\r\nGordon indulged in another fit of meditation, and then he said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow that leaves you at liberty to say what you please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, my dear fellow, you are ridiculous!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat \xe2\x80\x98s precisely what I want you to say. You always think me too\r\nreasonable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I go back to my first assertion. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know Miss Vivian--I mean\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t know her to have opinions about her. I don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose you wish\r\nme to string you off a dozen mere banalites--\xe2\x80\x98She \xe2\x80\x98s a charming\r\ngirl--evidently a superior person--has a great deal of style.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gordon; \xe2\x80\x9cI know all that. But, at any rate,\xe2\x80\x9d he added,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cyou like her, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do more,\xe2\x80\x9d said Longueville. \xe2\x80\x9cI admire her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that doing more?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Gordon, reflectively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, the greater, whichever it is, includes the less.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou won\xe2\x80\x99t commit yourself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gordon. \xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Bernard,\xe2\x80\x9d he added,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you knew such an immense deal about women!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGordon Wright was of so kindly and candid a nature that it is hardly\r\nconceivable that this remark should have been framed to make Bernard\r\ncommit himself by putting him on his mettle. Such a view would imply\r\nindeed on Gordon\xe2\x80\x99s part a greater familiarity with the uses of irony\r\nthan he had ever possessed, as well as a livelier conviction of the\r\nirritable nature of his friend\xe2\x80\x99s vanity. In fact, however, it may be\r\nconfided to the reader that Bernard was pricked in a tender place,\r\nthough the resentment of vanity was not visible in his answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were quite wrong,\xe2\x80\x9d he simply said. \xe2\x80\x9cI am as ignorant of women as a\r\nmonk in his cloister.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou try to prove too much. You don\xe2\x80\x99t think her sympathetic!\xe2\x80\x9d And as\r\nregards this last remark, Gordon Wright must be credited with a certain\r\nironical impulse.\r\n\r\nBernard stopped impatiently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ask you again, what does it matter to you what I think of her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt matters in this sense--that she has refused me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRefused you? Then it is all over, and nothing matters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it is n\xe2\x80\x99t over,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gordon, with a positive head-shake. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou see it is n\xe2\x80\x99t over?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard smiled, laid his hand on his friend\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder and patted it a\r\nlittle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour attitude might almost pass for that of resignation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI \xe2\x80\x98m not resigned!\xe2\x80\x9d said Gordon Wright.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course not. But when were you refused?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGordon stood a minute with his eyes fixed on the ground. Then, at last\r\nlooking up,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree weeks ago--a fortnight before you came. But let us walk along,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n he said, \xe2\x80\x9cand I will tell you all about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI proposed to her three weeks ago,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gordon, as they walked along.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy heart was very much set upon it. I was very hard hit--I was deeply\r\nsmitten. She had been very kind to me--she had been charming--I thought\r\nshe liked me. Then I thought her mother was pleased, and would have\r\nliked it. Mrs. Vivian, in fact, told me as much; for of course I spoke\r\nto her first. Well, Angela does like me--or at least she did--and I see\r\nno reason to suppose she has changed. Only she did n\xe2\x80\x99t like me enough.\r\nShe said the friendliest and pleasantest things to me, but she thought\r\nthat she knew me too little, and that I knew her even less. She made a\r\ngreat point of that--that I had no right, as yet, to trust her. I told\r\nher that if she would trust me, I was perfectly willing to trust her;\r\nbut she answered that this was poor reasoning. She said that I was\r\ntrustworthy and that she was not, and--in short, all sorts of nonsense.\r\nShe abused herself roundly--accused herself of no end of defects.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat defects, for instance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I have n\xe2\x80\x99t remembered them. She said she had a bad temper--that\r\nshe led her mother a dreadful life. Now, poor Mrs. Vivian says she is an\r\nangel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh yes,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard observed; \xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Vivian says that, very freely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAngela declared that she was jealous, ungenerous, unforgiving--all\r\nsorts of things. I remember she said \xe2\x80\x98I am very false,\xe2\x80\x99 and I think she\r\nremarked that she was cruel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut this did n\xe2\x80\x99t put you off,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all. She was making up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe makes up very well!\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard exclaimed, laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you call that well?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean it was very clever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was not clever from the point of view of wishing to discourage me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPossibly. But I am sure,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, \xe2\x80\x9cthat if I had been present at\r\nyour interview--excuse the impudence of the hypothesis--I should have\r\nbeen struck with the young lady\xe2\x80\x99s--\xe2\x80\x9d and he paused a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith her what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith her ability.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, her ability was not sufficient to induce me to give up my idea.\r\nShe told me that after I had known her six months I should detest her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no doubt she could make you do it if she should try. That \xe2\x80\x98s\r\nwhat I mean by her ability.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe calls herself cruel,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gordon, \xe2\x80\x9cbut she has not had the cruelty\r\nto try. She has been very reasonable--she has been perfect. I agreed\r\nwith her that I would drop the subject for a while, and that meanwhile\r\nwe should be good friends. We should take time to know each other better\r\nand act in accordance with further knowledge. There was no hurry, since\r\nwe trusted each other--wrong as my trust might be. She had no wish that\r\nI should go away. I was not in the least disagreeable to her; she liked\r\nme extremely, and I was perfectly free to try and please her. Only I\r\nshould drop my proposal, and be free to take it up again or leave it\r\nalone, later, as I should choose. If she felt differently then, I should\r\nhave the benefit of it, and if I myself felt differently, I should also\r\nhave the benefit of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat \xe2\x80\x98s a very comfortable arrangement. And that \xe2\x80\x98s your present\r\nsituation?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Bernard.\r\n\r\nGordon hesitated a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore or less, but not exactly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Vivian feels differently?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot that I know of.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGordon\xe2\x80\x99s companion, with a laugh, clapped him on the shoulder again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAdmirable youth, you are a capital match!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you alluding to my money?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo your money and to your modesty. There is as much of one as of the\r\nother--which is saying a great deal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gordon, \xe2\x80\x9cin spite of that enviable combination, I am not\r\nhappy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you seemed pensive!\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cIt \xe2\x80\x98s you, then, who\r\nfeel differently.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGordon gave a sigh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo say that is to say too much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat shall we say, then?\xe2\x80\x9d his companion asked, kindly.\r\n\r\nGordon stopped again; he stood there looking up at a certain\r\nparticularly lustrous star which twinkled--the night was cloudy--in an\r\nopen patch of sky, and the vague brightness shone down on his honest and\r\nserious visage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand her,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I \xe2\x80\x98ll say that with you any day!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bernard. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t help you\r\nthere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must help me;\xe2\x80\x9d and Gordon Wright deserted his star. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must keep\r\nme in good humor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease to walk on, then. I don\xe2\x80\x99t in the least pity you; she is very\r\ncharming with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue enough; but insisting on that is not the way to keep me in good\r\nhumor--when I feel as I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow is it you feel?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPuzzled to death--bewildered--depressed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was but the beginning of Gordon Wright\xe2\x80\x99s list; he went on to say\r\nthat though he \xe2\x80\x9cthought as highly\xe2\x80\x9d of Miss Vivian as he had ever done,\r\nhe felt less at his ease with her than in the first weeks of their\r\nacquaintance, and this condition made him uncomfortable and unhappy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what \xe2\x80\x98s the matter,\xe2\x80\x9d said poor Gordon. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what\r\nhas come between us. It is n\xe2\x80\x99t her fault--I don\xe2\x80\x99t make her responsible\r\nfor it. I began to notice it about a fortnight ago--before you came;\r\nshortly after that talk I had with her that I have just described to\r\nyou. Her manner has n\xe2\x80\x99t changed and I have no reason to suppose that\r\nshe likes me any the less; but she makes a strange impression on me--she\r\nmakes me uneasy. It \xe2\x80\x98s only her nature coming out, I suppose--what you\r\nmight call her originality. She \xe2\x80\x98s thoroughly original--she \xe2\x80\x98s a kind\r\nof mysterious creature. I suppose that what I feel is a sort of\r\nfascination; but that is just what I don\xe2\x80\x99t like. Hang it, I don\xe2\x80\x99t want\r\nto be fascinated--I object to being fascinated!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis little story had taken some time in the telling, so that the two\r\nyoung men had now reached their hotel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, my dear Gordon,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, \xe2\x80\x9cwe speak a different language. If\r\nyou don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be fascinated, what is one to say to you? \xe2\x80\x98Object to\r\nbeing fascinated!\xe2\x80\x99 There \xe2\x80\x98s a man easy to satisfy! Raffine, va!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, see here now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gordon, stopping in the door-way of the inn;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwhen it comes to the point, do you like it yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen it comes to the point?\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cI assure you I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwait till then. I like the beginning--I delight in the approach of it--I\r\nrevel in the prospect.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s just what I did. But now that the thing has come--I don\xe2\x80\x99t revel.\r\nTo be fascinated is to be mystified. Damn it, I like my liberty--I like\r\nmy judgment!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo do I--like yours,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, laughing, as they took their\r\nbedroom candles.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX\r\n\r\nBernard talked of this matter rather theoretically, inasmuch as to\r\nhis own sense, he was in a state neither of incipient nor of absorbed\r\nfascination. He got on very easily, however, with Angela Vivian, and\r\nfelt none of the mysterious discomfort alluded to by his friend. The\r\nelement of mystery attached itself rather to the young lady\xe2\x80\x99s mother,\r\nwho gave him the impression that for undiscoverable reasons she avoided\r\nhis society. He regretted her evasive deportment, for he found something\r\nagreeable in this shy and scrupulous little woman, who struck him as a\r\ncurious specimen of a society of which he had once been very fond. He\r\nlearned that she was of old New England stock, but he had not needed\r\nthis information to perceive that Mrs. Vivian was animated by the genius\r\nof Boston. \xe2\x80\x9cShe has the Boston temperament,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, using a\r\nphrase with which he had become familiar and which evoked a train of\r\nassociations. But then he immediately added that if Mrs. Vivian was a\r\ndaughter of the Puritans, the Puritan strain in her disposition had\r\nbeen mingled with another element. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is the Boston temperament\r\nsophisticated,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cperverted a little--perhaps even corrupted.\r\nIt is the local east-wind with an infusion from climates less tonic.\xe2\x80\x9d It\r\nseemed to him that Mrs. Vivian was a Puritan grown worldly--a Bostonian\r\nrelaxed; and this impression, oddly enough, contributed to his wish to\r\nknow more of her. He felt like going up to her very politely and saying,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear lady and most honored compatriot, what in the world have I done\r\nto displease you? You don\xe2\x80\x99t approve of me, and I am dying to know the\r\nreason why. I should be so happy to exert myself to be agreeable to you.\r\nIt \xe2\x80\x98s no use; you give me the cold shoulder. When I speak to you, you\r\nlook the other way; it is only when I speak to your daughter that you\r\nlook at me. It is true that at those times you look at me very hard, and\r\nif I am not greatly mistaken, you are not gratified by what you see.\r\nYou count the words I address to your beautiful Angela--you time our\r\nharmless little interviews. You interrupt them indeed whenever you can;\r\nyou call her away--you appeal to her; you cut across the conversation.\r\nYou are always laying plots to keep us apart. Why can\xe2\x80\x99t you leave me\r\nalone? I assure you I am the most innocent of men. Your beautiful Angela\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t possibly be injured by my conversation, and I have no designs\r\nwhatever upon her peace of mind. What on earth have I done to offend\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThese observations Bernard Longueville was disposed to make, and one\r\nafternoon, the opportunity offering, they rose to his lips and came very\r\nnear passing them. In fact, however, at the last moment, his eloquence\r\ntook another turn. It was the custom of the orchestra at the Kursaal\r\nto play in the afternoon, and as the music was often good, a great many\r\npeople assembled under the trees, at three o\xe2\x80\x99clock, to listen to it.\r\nThis was not, as a regular thing, an hour of re-union for the little\r\ngroup in which we are especially interested; Miss Vivian, in particular,\r\nunless an excursion of some sort had been agreed upon the day before,\r\nwas usually not to be seen in the precincts of the Conversation-house\r\nuntil the evening. Bernard, one afternoon, at three o\xe2\x80\x99clock, directed\r\nhis steps to this small world-centre of Baden, and, passing along the\r\nterrace, soon encountered little Blanche Evers strolling there under a\r\npink parasol and accompanied by Captain Lovelock. This young lady was\r\nalways extremely sociable; it was quite in accordance with her habitual\r\ngeniality that she should stop and say how d\xe2\x80\x99 ye do to our hero.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Longueville is growing very frivolous,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9ccoming to the\r\nKursaal at all sorts of hours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is nothing frivolous in coming here with the hope of finding\r\nyou,\xe2\x80\x9d the young man answered. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is very serious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be more serious to lose Miss Evers than to find her,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked\r\nCaptain Lovelock, with gallant jocosity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish you would lose me!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the young girl. \xe2\x80\x9cI think I should like\r\nto be lost. I might have all kinds of adventures.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI \xe2\x80\x98guess\xe2\x80\x99 so!\xe2\x80\x9d said Captain Lovelock, hilariously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I should find my way. I can take care of myself!\xe2\x80\x9d Blanche went on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Vivian does n\xe2\x80\x99t think so,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, who had just perceived\r\nthis lady, seated under a tree with a book, over the top of which she\r\nwas observing her pretty protege. Blanche looked toward her and gave her\r\na little nod and a smile. Then chattering on to the young men--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe \xe2\x80\x98s awfully careful. I never saw any one so careful. But I\r\nsuppose she is right. She promised my mother she would be tremendously\r\nparticular; but I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what she thinks I would do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is n\xe2\x80\x99t flattering to me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Captain Lovelock. \xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Vivian\r\ndoes n\xe2\x80\x99t approve of me--she wishes me in Jamaica. What does she think me\r\ncapable of?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd me, now?\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard asked. \xe2\x80\x9cShe likes me least of all, and I, on my\r\nside, think she \xe2\x80\x98s so nice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t say I \xe2\x80\x98m very sweet on her,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Captain. \xe2\x80\x9cShe strikes me as\r\nfeline.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBlanche Evers gave a little cry of horror.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop, sir, this instant! I won\xe2\x80\x99t have you talk that way about a lady\r\nwho has been so kind to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is n\xe2\x80\x99t so kind to you. She would like to lock you up where I can\r\nnever see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI \xe2\x80\x98m sure I should n\xe2\x80\x99t mind that!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the young girl, with a\r\nlittle laugh and a toss of her head. \xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Vivian has the most perfect\r\ncharacter--that \xe2\x80\x98s why my mother wanted me to come with her. And if she\r\npromised my mother she would be careful, is n\xe2\x80\x99t she right to keep her\r\npromise? She \xe2\x80\x98s a great deal more careful than mamma ever was, and that\r\n\xe2\x80\x98s just what mamma wanted. She would never take the trouble herself. And\r\nthen she was always scolding me. Mrs. Vivian never scolds me. She only\r\nwatches me, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish she would watch you a little less and scold you a little more,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Captain Lovelock.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no doubt you wish a great many horrid things,\xe2\x80\x9d his companion\r\nrejoined, with delightful asperity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, unfortunately I never have anything I wish!\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Lovelock.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour wishes must be comprehensive,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard. \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to me you\r\nhave a good deal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Englishman gave a shrug.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt \xe2\x80\x98s less than you might think. She is watching us more furiously than\r\never,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, in a moment, looking at Mrs. Vivian. \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Gordon Wright\r\nis the only man she likes. She is awfully fond of Mr. Gordon Wright.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Mrs. Vivian shows her wisdom!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is certainly very handsome,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Blanche Evers, glancing\r\nseveral times, with a very pretty aggressiveness, at Captain Lovelock.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must say I like Mr. Gordon Wright. Why in the world did you come here\r\nwithout him?\xe2\x80\x9d she went on, addressing herself to Bernard. \xe2\x80\x9cYou two are\r\nso awfully inseparable. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I ever saw you alone before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I have often seen Mr. Gordon Wright alone,\xe2\x80\x9d said Captain\r\nLovelock--\xe2\x80\x9cthat is, alone with Miss Vivian. That \xe2\x80\x98s what the old lady\r\nlikes; she can\xe2\x80\x99t have too much of that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young girl, poised for an instant in one of her pretty attitudes,\r\nlooked at him from head to foot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I call that scandalous! Do you mean that she wants to make a\r\nmatch?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean that the young man has six thousand a year.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt \xe2\x80\x98s no matter what he has--six thousand a year is n\xe2\x80\x99t much! And we\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t do things in that way in our country. We have n\xe2\x80\x99t those horrid\r\nmatch-making arrangements that you have in your dreadful country.\r\nAmerican mothers are not like English mothers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, any one can see, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d said Captain Lovelock, \xe2\x80\x9cthat Mr.\r\nGordon Wright is dying of love for Miss Vivian.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t see it!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Blanche.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe dies easier than I, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish you would die!\xe2\x80\x9d said Blanche. \xe2\x80\x9cAt any rate, Angela is not dying\r\nof love for Mr. Wright.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, she will marry him all the same,\xe2\x80\x9d Lovelock declared.\r\n\r\nBlanche Evers glanced at Bernard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you contradict that?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you speak up for\r\nyour friend?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am quite ready to speak for my friend,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I am not\r\nready to speak for Miss Vivian.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I am,\xe2\x80\x9d Blanche declared. \xe2\x80\x9cShe won\xe2\x80\x99t marry him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf she does n\xe2\x80\x99t, I \xe2\x80\x98ll eat my hat!\xe2\x80\x9d said Captain Lovelock. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do\r\nyou mean,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, \xe2\x80\x9cby saying that in America a pretty girl\xe2\x80\x99s mother\r\ndoes n\xe2\x80\x99t care for a young fellow\xe2\x80\x99s property?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, they don\xe2\x80\x99t--we consider that dreadful. Why don\xe2\x80\x99t you say so,\r\nMr. Longueville?\xe2\x80\x9d Blanche demanded. \xe2\x80\x9cI never saw any one take things so\r\nquietly. Have n\xe2\x80\x99t you got any patriotism?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy patriotism is modified by an indisposition to generalize,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nBernard, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cOn this point permit me not to generalize. I am\r\ninterested in the particular case--in ascertaining whether Mrs. Vivian\r\nthinks very often of Gordon Wright\xe2\x80\x99s income.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Evers gave a little toss of disgust.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you are so awfully impartial, you had better go and ask her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat \xe2\x80\x98s a good idea--I think I will go and ask her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard.\r\n\r\nCaptain Lovelock returned to his argument.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean to say that your mother would be indifferent to the fact\r\nthat I have n\xe2\x80\x99t a shilling in the world?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndifferent?\xe2\x80\x9d Blanche demanded. \xe2\x80\x9cOh no, she would be sorry for you. She\r\nis very charitable--she would give you a shilling!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe would n\xe2\x80\x99t let you marry me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lovelock.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe would n\xe2\x80\x99t have much trouble to prevent it!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the young girl.\r\n\r\nBernard had had enough of this intellectual fencing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I will go and ask Mrs. Vivian,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated. And he left his\r\ncompanions to resume their walk.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X\r\n\r\nIt had seemed to him a good idea to interrogate Mrs. Vivian; but there\r\nare a great many good ideas that are never put into execution. As he\r\napproached her with a smile and a salutation, and, with the air of\r\nasking leave to take a liberty, seated himself in the empty chair beside\r\nher, he felt a humorous relish of her own probable dismay which relaxed\r\nthe investigating impulse. His impulse was now simply to prove to her\r\nthat he was the most unobjectionable fellow in the world--a proposition\r\nwhich resolved itself into several ingenious observations upon the\r\nweather, the music, the charms and the drawbacks of Baden, the merits of\r\nthe volume that she held in her lap. If Mrs. Vivian should be annoyed,\r\nshould be fluttered, Bernard would feel very sorry for her; there was\r\nnothing in the world that he respected more than the moral consciousness\r\nof a little Boston woman whose view of life was serious and whose\r\nimagination was subject to alarms. He held it to be a temple of\r\ndelicacy, where one should walk on tiptoe, and he wished to exhibit\r\nto Mrs. Vivian the possible lightness of his own step. She herself\r\nwas incapable of being rude or ungracious, and now that she was fairly\r\nconfronted with the plausible object of her mistrust, she composed\r\nherself to her usual attitude of refined liberality. Her book was a\r\nvolume of Victor Cousin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must have an extraordinary power of abstracting your mind,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard\r\nsaid to her, observing it. \xe2\x80\x9cStudying philosophy at the Baden Kursaal\r\nstrikes me as a real intellectual feat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you think we need a little philosophy here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy all means--what we bring with us. But I should n\xe2\x80\x99t attempt the use\r\nof the text-book on the spot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should n\xe2\x80\x99t speak of yourself as if you were not clever,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs.\r\nVivian. \xe2\x80\x9cEvery one says you are so very clever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLongueville stared; there was an unexpectedness in the speech and an\r\nincongruity in Mrs. Vivian\xe2\x80\x99s beginning to flatter him. He needed\r\nto remind himself that if she was a Bostonian, she was a Bostonian\r\nperverted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, my dear madam, every one is no one,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was Mr. Wright, in particular,\xe2\x80\x9d she rejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cHe has always told us\r\nthat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is blinded by friendship.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh yes, we know about your friendship,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Vivian. \xe2\x80\x9cHe has told\r\nus about that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are making him out a terrible talker!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe think he talks so well--we are so very fond of his conversation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt \xe2\x80\x98s usually excellent,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it depends a good deal on\r\nthe subject.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Mrs. Vivian, \xe2\x80\x9cwe always let him choose his subjects.\xe2\x80\x9d And\r\ndropping her eyes as if in sudden reflection, she began to smooth down\r\nthe crumpled corner of her volume.\r\n\r\nIt occurred to Bernard that--by some mysterious impulse--she was\r\nsuddenly presenting him with a chance to ask her the question that\r\nBlanche Evers had just suggested. Two or three other things as well\r\noccurred to him. Captain Lovelock had been struck with the fact that she\r\nfavored Gordon Wright\xe2\x80\x99s addresses to her daughter, and Captain Lovelock\r\nhad a grotesque theory that she had set her heart upon seeing this young\r\nlady come into six thousand a year. Miss Evers\xe2\x80\x99s devoted swain had never\r\nstruck Bernard as a brilliant reasoner, but our friend suddenly found\r\nhimself regarding him as one of the inspired. The form of depravity into\r\nwhich the New England conscience had lapsed on Mrs. Vivian\xe2\x80\x99s part was\r\nan undue appreciation of a possible son-in-law\xe2\x80\x99s income! In this\r\nilluminating discovery everything else became clear. Mrs. Vivian\r\ndisliked her humble servant because he had not thirty thousand dollars\r\na year, and because at a moment when it was Angela\xe2\x80\x99s prime duty to\r\nconcentrate her thoughts upon Gordon Wright\xe2\x80\x99s great advantages, a clever\r\nyoung man of paltry fortune was a superfluous diversion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen you say clever, everything is relative,\xe2\x80\x9d he presently observed.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, there is Captain Lovelock; he has a certain kind of cleverness; he\r\nis very observant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Vivian glanced up with a preoccupied air.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe don\xe2\x80\x99t like Captain Lovelock,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have heard him say capital things,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe think him brutal,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Vivian. \xe2\x80\x9cPlease don\xe2\x80\x99t praise Captain\r\nLovelock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I only want to be just.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Vivian for a moment said nothing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you want very much to be just?\xe2\x80\x9d she presently asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt \xe2\x80\x98s my most ardent desire.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI \xe2\x80\x98m glad to hear that--and I can easily believe it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Vivian.\r\n\r\nBernard gave her a grateful smile, but while he smiled, he asked himself\r\na serious question. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy the deuce does she go on flattering me?--You\r\nhave always been very kind to me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said aloud.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt \xe2\x80\x98s on Mr. Wright\xe2\x80\x99s account,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered demurely.\r\n\r\nIn speaking the words I have just quoted, Bernard Longueville had felt\r\nhimself, with a certain compunction, to be skirting the edge of clever\r\nimpudence; but Mrs. Vivian\xe2\x80\x99s quiet little reply suggested to him that\r\nher cleverness, if not her impudence, was almost equal to his own. He\r\nremarked to himself that he had not yet done her justice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou bring everything back to Gordon Wright,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, continuing to\r\nsmile.\r\n\r\nMrs. Vivian blushed a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is because he is really at the foundation of everything that is\r\npleasant for us here. When we first came we had some very disagreeable\r\nrooms, and as soon as he arrived he found us some excellent ones--that\r\nwere less expensive. And then, Mr. Longueville,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, with a\r\nsoft, sweet emphasis which should properly have contradicted the idea\r\nof audacity, but which, to Bernard\xe2\x80\x99s awakened sense, seemed really to\r\nimpart a vivid color to it, \xe2\x80\x9che was also the cause of your joining our\r\nlittle party.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, among his services that should never be forgotten. You should set\r\nup a tablet to commemorate it, in the wall of the Kursaal!--The wicked\r\nlittle woman!\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard mentally subjoined.\r\n\r\nMrs. Vivian appeared quite unruffled by his sportive sarcasm, and she\r\ncontinued to enumerate her obligations to Gordon Wright.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are so many ways in which a gentleman can be of assistance to\r\nthree poor lonely women, especially when he is at the same time so\r\nfriendly and so delicate as Mr. Wright. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what we should have\r\ndone without him, and I feel as if every one ought to know it. He seems\r\nlike a very old friend. My daughter and I quite worship him. I will not\r\nconceal from you that when I saw you coming through the grounds a short\r\ntime ago without him I was very much disappointed. I hope he is not\r\nill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard sat listening, with his eyes on the ground.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, he is simply at home writing letters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Vivian was silent a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose he has a very large correspondence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI really don\xe2\x80\x99t know. Just now that I am with him he has a smaller one\r\nthan usual.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh yes. When you are separated I suppose you write volumes to each\r\nother. But he must have a great many business letters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very likely,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd if he has, you may be sure he\r\nwrites them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOrder and method!\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Vivian exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cWith his immense property\r\nthose virtues are necessary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard glanced at her a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Lovelock,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are not such a fool as you\r\nseem.--Gordon\xe2\x80\x99s virtues are always necessary, doubtless,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut should you say his property was immense?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Vivian made a delicate little movement of deprecation. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nask me to say! I know nothing about it; I only supposed he was rich.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is rich; but he is not a Croesus.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you fashionable young men have a standard of luxury!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs.\r\nVivian, with a little laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cTo a poverty-stricken widow such a fortune\r\nas Mr. Wright\xe2\x80\x99s seems magnificent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t call me such horrible names!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Bernard. \xe2\x80\x9cOur friend has\r\ncertainly money enough and to spare.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was all I meant. He once had occasion to allude to his property,\r\nbut he was so modest, so reserved in the tone he took about it, that one\r\nhardly knew what to think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is ashamed of being rich,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard. \xe2\x80\x9cHe would be sure to\r\nrepresent everything unfavorably.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat \xe2\x80\x98s just what I thought!\xe2\x80\x9d This ejaculation was more eager than Mrs.\r\nVivian might have intended, but even had it been less so, Bernard was in\r\na mood to appreciate it. \xe2\x80\x9cI felt that we should make allowances for his\r\nmodesty. But it was in very good taste,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Vivian added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe \xe2\x80\x98s a fortunate man,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard. \xe2\x80\x9cHe gets credit for his good\r\ntaste--and he gets credit for the full figure of his income as well!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Mrs. Vivian, rising lightly, as if to make her words\r\nappear more casual, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know the full figure of his income.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was turning away, and Bernard, as he raised his hat and separated\r\nfrom her, felt that it was rather cruel that he should let her go\r\nwithout enlightening her ignorance. But he said to himself that she knew\r\nquite enough. Indeed, he took a walk along the Lichtenthal Alley and\r\ncarried out this line of reflection. Whether or no Miss Vivian were in\r\nlove with Gordon Wright, her mother was enamored of Gordon\xe2\x80\x99s fortune,\r\nand it had suddenly occurred to her that instead of treating the friend\r\nof her daughter\xe2\x80\x99s suitor with civil mistrust, she would help her case\r\nbetter by giving him a hint of her state of mind and appealing to his\r\nsense of propriety. Nothing could be more natural than that Mrs. Vivian\r\nshould suppose that Bernard desired his friend\xe2\x80\x99s success; for, as our\r\nthoughtful hero said to himself, what she had hitherto taken it into her\r\nhead to fear was not that Bernard should fall in love with her daughter,\r\nbut that her daughter should fall in love with him. Watering-place life\r\nis notoriously conducive to idleness of mind, and Bernard strolled for\r\nhalf an hour along the overarched avenue, glancing alternately at these\r\ntwo insupposable cases.\r\n\r\nA few days afterward, late in the evening, Gordon Wright came to his\r\nroom at the hotel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have just received a letter from my sister,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid I\r\nshall have to go away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, I \xe2\x80\x98m sorry for that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, who was so well pleased with\r\nthe actual that he desired no mutation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean only for a short time,\xe2\x80\x9d Gordon explained. \xe2\x80\x9cMy poor sister writes\r\nfrom England, telling me that my brother-in-law is suddenly obliged to\r\ngo home. She has decided not to remain behind, and they are to sail a\r\nfortnight hence. She wants very much to see me before she goes, and as I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know when I shall see her again, I feel as if I ought to join\r\nher immediately and spend the interval with her. That will take about a\r\nfortnight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI appreciate the sanctity of family ties and I project myself into\r\nyour situation,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard. \xe2\x80\x9cOn the other hand, I don\xe2\x80\x99t envy you a\r\nbreathless journey from Baden to Folkestone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt \xe2\x80\x98s the coming back that will be breathless,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Gordon,\r\nsmiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will certainly come back, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMost certainly. Mrs. Vivian is to be here another month.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand. Well, we shall miss you very much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGordon Wright looked for a moment at his companion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will stay here, then? I am so glad of that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was taking it for granted; but on reflection--what do you recommend?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI recommend you to stay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear fellow, your word is law,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want you to take care of those ladies,\xe2\x80\x9d his friend went on. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlike to leave them alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are joking!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bernard. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen did you ever hear of my \xe2\x80\x98taking\r\ncare\xe2\x80\x99 of any one? It \xe2\x80\x98s as much as I can do to take care of myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is very easy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gordon. \xe2\x80\x9cI simply want to feel that they have\r\na man about them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey will have a man at any rate--they have the devoted Lovelock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat \xe2\x80\x98s just why I want them to have another. He has only an eye to\r\nMiss Evers, who, by the way, is extremely bored with him. You look after\r\nthe others. You have made yourself very agreeable to them, and they like\r\nyou extremely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, laughing, \xe2\x80\x9cif you are going to be coarse and\r\nflattering, I collapse. If you are going to titillate my vanity, I\r\nsuccumb.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt won\xe2\x80\x99t be so disagreeable,\xe2\x80\x9d Gordon observed, with an intention\r\nvaguely humorous.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, it won\xe2\x80\x99t be disagreeable. I will go to Mrs. Vivian every\r\nmorning, hat in hand, for my orders.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGordon Wright, with his hands in his pockets and a meditative\r\nexpression, took several turns about the room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will be a capital chance,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, at last, stopping in front of\r\nhis companion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA chance for what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA chance to arrive at a conclusion about my young friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard gave a gentle groan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you coming back to that? Did n\xe2\x80\x99t I arrive at a conclusion long ago?\r\nDid n\xe2\x80\x99t I tell you she was a delightful girl?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you call that a conclusion? The first comer could tell me that at\r\nthe end of an hour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you want me to invent something different?\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard asked. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ninvent anything better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want you to invent anything. I only want you to observe her--to\r\nstudy her in complete independence. You will have her to yourself--my\r\nabsence will leave you at liberty. Hang it, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d Gordon declared, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nshould think you would like it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDamn it, sir, you \xe2\x80\x98re delicious!\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard answered; and he broke into\r\nan irrepressible laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose it \xe2\x80\x98s for my pleasure that you\r\nsuggest the arrangement.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGordon took a turn about the room again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it \xe2\x80\x98s for mine. At least, it \xe2\x80\x98s for my benefit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor your benefit?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have got all sorts of ideas--I told you the other day. They are all\r\nmixed up together and I want a fresh impression.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy impressions are never fresh,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey would be if you had a little good-will--if you entered a little\r\ninto my dilemma.\xe2\x80\x9d The note of reproach was so distinct in these words\r\nthat Bernard stood staring. \xe2\x80\x9cYou never take anything seriously,\xe2\x80\x9d his\r\ncompanion went on.\r\n\r\nBernard tried to answer as seriously as possible.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour dilemma seems to me of all dilemmas the strangest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat may be; but different people take things differently. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nsee,\xe2\x80\x9d Gordon went on with a sudden outbreak of passion--\xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nsee that I am horribly divided in mind? I care immensely for Angela\r\nVivian--and yet--and yet--I am afraid of her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfraid of her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid she \xe2\x80\x98s cleverer than I--that she would be a difficult wife;\r\nthat she might do strange things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat sort of things?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that she might flirt, for instance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat \xe2\x80\x98s not a thing for a man to fear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot when he supposes his wife to be fond of him--no. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsuppose that--I have given that up. If I should induce Angela Vivian to\r\naccept me she would do it on grounds purely reasonable. She would think\r\nit best, simply. That would give her a chance to repent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard sat for some time looking at his friend.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou say she is cleverer than you. It \xe2\x80\x98s impossible to be cleverer than\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, come, Longueville!\xe2\x80\x9d said Gordon, angrily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am speaking very seriously. You have done a remarkably clever thing.\r\nYou have impressed me with the reality, and with--what shall I term\r\nit?--the estimable character of what you call your dilemma. Now this\r\nfresh impression of mine--what do you propose to do with it when you get\r\nit?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuch things are always useful. It will be a good thing to have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am much obliged to you; but do you propose to let anything depend\r\nupon it? Do you propose to take or to leave Miss Vivian--that is, to\r\nreturn to the charge or to give up trying--in consequence of my fresh\r\nimpression?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGordon seemed perfectly unembarrassed by this question, in spite of the\r\nironical light which it projected upon his sentimental perplexity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI propose to do what I choose!\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat \xe2\x80\x98s a relief to me,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard rejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cThis idea of yours is,\r\nafter all, only the play of the scientific mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall contradict you flat if I choose,\xe2\x80\x9d Gordon went on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, it \xe2\x80\x98s well to warn me of that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cEven the\r\nmost sincere judgment in the world likes to be notified a little of the\r\ndanger of being contradicted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs yours the most sincere judgment in the world?\xe2\x80\x9d Gordon demanded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat \xe2\x80\x98s a very pertinent question. Does n\xe2\x80\x99t it occur to you that you\r\nmay have reason to be jealous--leaving me alone, with an open field,\r\nwith the woman of your choice?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish to heaven I could be jealous!\xe2\x80\x9d Gordon exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cThat would\r\nsimplify the thing--that would give me a lift.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd the next day, after some more talk, it seemed really with a hope of\r\nthis contingency--though, indeed, he laughed about it--that he started\r\nfor England.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI\r\n\r\nFor the three or four days that followed Gordon Wright\xe2\x80\x99s departure,\r\nBernard saw nothing of the ladies who had been committed to his charge.\r\nThey chose to remain in seclusion, and he was at liberty to interpret\r\nthis fact as an expression of regret at the loss of Gordon\xe2\x80\x99s good\r\noffices. He knew other people at Baden, and he went to see them and\r\nendeavored, by cultivating their society, to await in patience the\r\nre-appearance of Mrs. Vivian and her companions. But on the fourth day\r\nhe became conscious that other people were much less interesting than\r\nthe trio of American ladies who had lodgings above the confectioner\xe2\x80\x99s,\r\nand he made bold to go and knock at their door. He had been asked to\r\ntake care of them, and this function presupposed contact. He had\r\nmet Captain Lovelock the day before, wandering about with a rather\r\ncrest-fallen aspect, and the young Englishman had questioned him eagerly\r\nas to the whereabouts of Mrs. Vivian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGad, I believe they \xe2\x80\x98ve left the place--left the place without giving a\r\nfellow warning!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Lovelock.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, I think they are here still,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard. \xe2\x80\x9cMy friend Wright\r\nhas gone away for a week or two, but I suspect the ladies are simply\r\nstaying at home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGad, I was afraid your friend Wright had taken them away with him; he\r\nseems to keep them all in his pocket. I was afraid he had given them\r\nmarching orders; they \xe2\x80\x98d have been sure to go--they \xe2\x80\x98re so awfully fond\r\nof his pocket! I went to look them up yesterday--upon my word I did.\r\nThey live at a baker\xe2\x80\x99s in a little back-street; people do live in rum\r\nplaces when they come abroad! But I assure you, when I got there, I \xe2\x80\x98m\r\ndamned if I could make out whether they were there or not. I don\xe2\x80\x99t speak\r\na word of German, and there was no one there but the baker\xe2\x80\x99s wife. She\r\nwas a low brute of a woman--she could n\xe2\x80\x99t understand a word I said,\r\nthough she gave me plenty of her own tongue. I had to give it up. They\r\nwere not at home, but whether they had left Baden or not--that was\r\nbeyond my finding out. If they are here, why the deuce don\xe2\x80\x99t they show?\r\nFancy coming to Baden-Baden to sit moping at a pastry-cook\xe2\x80\x99s!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCaptain Lovelock was evidently irritated, and it was Bernard\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nimpression that the turn of luck over yonder where the gold-pieces were\r\nchinking had something to do with the state of his temper. But more\r\nfortunate himself, he ascertained from the baker\xe2\x80\x99s wife that though Mrs.\r\nVivian and her daughter had gone out, their companion, \xe2\x80\x9cthe youngest\r\nlady--the little young lady\xe2\x80\x9d--was above in the sitting-room.\r\n\r\nBlanche Evers was sitting at the window with a book, but she\r\nrelinquished the volume with an alacrity that showed it had not been\r\nabsorbing, and began to chatter with her customary frankness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I must say I am glad to see some one!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the young girl,\r\npassing before the mirror and giving a touch to her charming tresses.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven if it \xe2\x80\x98s only me,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard exclaimed, laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did n\xe2\x80\x99t mean that. I am sure I am very glad to see you--I should\r\nthink you would have found out that by this time. I mean I \xe2\x80\x98m glad to\r\nsee any one--especially a man. I suppose it \xe2\x80\x98s improper for me to say\r\nthat--especially to you! There--you see I do think more of you than of\r\nsome gentlemen. Why especially to you? Well, because you always seem to\r\nme to want to take advantage. I did n\xe2\x80\x99t say a base advantage; I did n\xe2\x80\x99t\r\naccuse you of anything dreadful. I \xe2\x80\x98m sure I want to take advantage,\r\ntoo--I take it whenever I can. You see I take advantage of your being\r\nhere--I \xe2\x80\x98ve got so many things to say. I have n\xe2\x80\x99t spoken a word in three\r\ndays, and I \xe2\x80\x98m sure it is a pleasant change--a gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s visit. All of\r\na sudden we have gone into mourning; I \xe2\x80\x98m sure I don\xe2\x80\x99t know who \xe2\x80\x98s dead.\r\nIs it Mr. Gordon Wright? It \xe2\x80\x98s some idea of Mrs. Vivian\xe2\x80\x99s--I \xe2\x80\x98m sure\r\nit is n\xe2\x80\x99t mine. She thinks we have been often enough to the Kursaal. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know whether she thinks it \xe2\x80\x98s wicked, or what. If it \xe2\x80\x98s wicked the\r\nharm \xe2\x80\x98s already done; I can\xe2\x80\x99t be any worse than I am now. I have seen\r\nall the improper people and I have learnt all their names; Captain\r\nLovelock has told me their names, plenty of times. I don\xe2\x80\x99t see what good\r\nit does me to be shut up here with all those names running in my ears.\r\nI must say I do prefer society. We have n\xe2\x80\x99t been to the Kursaal for\r\nfour days--we have only gone out for a drive. We have taken the most\r\ninterminable drives. I do believe we have seen every old ruin in\r\nthe whole country. Mrs. Vivian and Angela are so awfully fond of\r\nscenery--they talk about it by the half-hour. They talk about the\r\nmountains and trees as if they were people they knew--as if they were\r\ngentlemen! I mean as if the mountains and trees were gentlemen. Of\r\ncourse scenery \xe2\x80\x98s lovely, but you can\xe2\x80\x99t walk about with a tree. At any\r\nrate, that has been all our society--foliage! Foliage and women; but I\r\nsuppose women are a sort of foliage. They are always rustling about and\r\ndropping off. That \xe2\x80\x98s why I could n\xe2\x80\x99t make up my mind to go out\r\nwith them this afternoon. They \xe2\x80\x98ve gone to see the Waterworths--the\r\nWaterworths arrived yesterday and are staying at some hotel. Five\r\ndaughters--all unmarried! I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what kind of foliage they are;\r\nsome peculiar kind--they don\xe2\x80\x99t drop off. I thought I had had about\r\nenough ladies\xe2\x80\x99 society--three women all sticking together! I don\xe2\x80\x99t think\r\nit \xe2\x80\x98s good for a young girl to have nothing but ladies\xe2\x80\x99 society--it \xe2\x80\x98s\r\nso awfully limited. I suppose I ought to stand up for my own sex and\r\ntell you that when we are alone together we want for nothing. But we\r\nwant for everything, as it happens! Women\xe2\x80\x99s talk is limited--every one\r\nknows that. That \xe2\x80\x98s just what mamma did n\xe2\x80\x99t want when she asked Mrs.\r\nVivian to take charge of me. Now, Mr. Longueville, what are you laughing\r\nat?--you are always laughing at me. She wanted me to be unlimited--is\r\nthat what you say? Well, she did n\xe2\x80\x99t want me to be narrowed down; she\r\nwanted me to have plenty of conversation. She wanted me to be fitted\r\nfor society--that \xe2\x80\x98s what mamma wanted. She wanted me to have ease of\r\nmanner; she thinks that if you don\xe2\x80\x99t acquire it when you are young you\r\nnever have it at all. She was so happy to think I should come to Baden;\r\nbut she would n\xe2\x80\x99t approve of the life I \xe2\x80\x98ve been leading the last four\r\ndays. That \xe2\x80\x98s no way to acquire ease of manner--sitting all day in a\r\nsmall parlor with two persons of one\xe2\x80\x99s own sex! Of course Mrs. Vivian\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ninfluence--that \xe2\x80\x98s the great thing. Mamma said it was like the odor of\r\na flower. But you don\xe2\x80\x99t want to keep smelling a flower all day, even\r\nthe sweetest; that \xe2\x80\x98s the shortest way to get a headache. Apropos of\r\nflowers, do you happen to have heard whether Captain Lovelock is alive\r\nor dead? Do I call him a flower? No; I call him a flower-pot. He always\r\nhas some fine young plant in his button-hole. He has n\xe2\x80\x99t been near me\r\nthese ten years--I never heard of anything so rude!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCaptain Lovelock came on the morrow, Bernard finding him in Mrs.\r\nVivian\xe2\x80\x99s little sitting-room on paying a second visit. On this occasion\r\nthe two other ladies were at home and Bernard was not exclusively\r\nindebted to Miss Evers for entertainment. It was to this source of\r\nhospitality, however, that Lovelock mainly appealed, following the\r\nyoung girl out upon the little balcony that was suspended above the\r\nconfectioner\xe2\x80\x99s window. Mrs. Vivian sat writing at one of the windows of\r\nthe sitting-room, and Bernard addressed his conversation to Angela.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWright requested me to keep an eye on you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cbut you seem very\r\nmuch inclined to keep out of my jurisdiction.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI supposed you had gone away,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered--\xe2\x80\x9cnow that your friend is\r\ngone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy no means. Gordon is a charming fellow, but he is by no means the\r\nonly attraction of Baden. Besides, I have promised him to look after\r\nyou--to take care of you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe girl looked at him a moment in silence--a little askance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you had probably undertaken something of that sort,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\npresently said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was of course a very natural request for Gordon to make.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAngela got up and turned away; she wandered about the room and went and\r\nstood at one of the windows. Bernard found the movement abrupt and\r\nnot particularly gracious; but the young man was not easy to snub. He\r\nfollowed her, and they stood at the second window--the long window that\r\nopened upon the balcony. Miss Evers and Captain Lovelock were leaning on\r\nthe railing, looking into the street and apparently amusing themselves\r\nhighly with what they saw.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not sure it was a natural request for him to make,\xe2\x80\x9d said Angela.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat could have been more so--devoted as he is to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe hesitated a moment; then with a little laugh--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe ought to have locked us up and said nothing about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt \xe2\x80\x98s not so easy to lock you up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard. \xe2\x80\x9cI know Wright has\r\ngreat influence with you, but you are after all independent beings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not an independent being. If my mother and Mr. Wright were to\r\nagree together to put me out of harm\xe2\x80\x99s way they could easily manage it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou seem to have been trying something of that sort,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have been so terribly invisible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was because I thought you had designs upon us; that you were\r\nwatching for us--to take care of us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou contradict yourself! You said just now that you believed I had left\r\nBaden.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was an artificial--a conventional speech. Is n\xe2\x80\x99t a lady always\r\nsupposed to say something of that sort to a visitor by way of pretending\r\nto have noticed that she has not seen him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know I would never have left Baden without coming to bid you\r\ngood-bye,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard.\r\n\r\nThe girl made no rejoinder; she stood looking out at the little sunny,\r\nslanting, rough-paved German street.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you taking care of us now?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked in a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cHas the\r\noperation begun? Have you heard the news, mamma?\xe2\x80\x9d she went on. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you\r\nknow that Mr. Wright has made us over to Mr. Longueville, to be kept\r\ntill called for? Suppose Mr. Wright should never call for us!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Vivian left her writing-table and came toward Bernard, smiling at\r\nhim and pressing her hands together.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no fear of that, I think,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI am sure I am very glad\r\nwe have a gentleman near us.
