The project of leaving\r\nBlanquais-les-Galets at nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the morning dropped lightly from\r\nhis mind, making no noise as it fell; but another took its place, which\r\nhad an air of being still more excellent and which consisted of starting\r\noff on a long walk and absenting himself for the day. Bernard grasped\r\nhis stick and wandered away; he climbed the great shoulder of the\r\nfurther cliff and found himself on the level downs. Here there was\r\napparently no obstacle whatever to his walking as far as his fancy\r\nshould carry him. The summer was still in a splendid mood, and the hot\r\nand quiet day--it was a Sunday--seemed to constitute a deep, silent\r\nsmile on the face of nature. The sea glistened on one side, and the\r\ncrops ripened on the other; the larks, losing themselves in the dense\r\nsunshine, made it ring here and there in undiscoverable spots; this\r\nwas the only sound save when Bernard, pausing now and then in his walk,\r\nfound himself hearing far below him, at the base of the cliff, the\r\ndrawling murmur of a wave. He walked a great many miles and passed\r\nthrough half a dozen of those rude fishing-hamlets, lodged in some\r\nsloping hollow of the cliffs, so many of which, of late years, all along\r\nthe Norman coast, have adorned themselves with a couple of hotels and a\r\nrow of bathing-machines. He walked so far that the shadows had begun to\r\nlengthen before he bethought himself of stopping; the afternoon had\r\ncome on and had already begun to wane. The grassy downs still stretched\r\nbefore him, shaded here and there with shallow but windless dells. He\r\nlooked for the softest place and then flung himself down on the grass;\r\nhe lay there for a long time, thinking of many things. He had determined\r\nto give himself up to a day\xe2\x80\x99s happiness; it was happiness of a\r\nvery harmless kind--the satisfaction of thought, the bliss of mere\r\nconsciousness; but such as it was it did not elude him nor turn bitter\r\nin his heart, and the long summer day closed upon him before his spirit,\r\nhovering in perpetual circles round the idea of what might be, had begun\r\nto rest its wing. When he rose to his feet again it was too late to\r\nreturn to Blanquais in the same way that he had come; the evening was\r\nat hand, the light was already fading, and the walk he had taken was\r\none which even if he had not felt very tired, he would have thought it\r\nimprudent to attempt to repeat in the darkness. He made his way to the\r\nnearest village, where he was able to hire a rustic carriole, in which\r\nprimitive conveyance, gaining the high-road, he jogged and jostled\r\nthrough the hours of the evening slowly back to his starting-point. It\r\nwanted an hour of midnight by the time he reached his inn, and there was\r\nnothing left for him but to go to bed.\r\n\r\nHe went in the unshaken faith that he should leave Blanquais early on\r\nthe morrow. But early on the morrow it occurred to him that it would be\r\nsimply grotesque to go off without taking leave of Mrs. Vivian and her\r\ndaughter, and offering them some explanation of his intention. He had\r\ngiven them to understand that, so delighted was he to find them there,\r\nhe would remain at Blanquais at least as long as they. He must have\r\nseemed to them wanting in civility, to spend a whole bright Sunday\r\nwithout apparently troubling his head about them, and if the unlucky\r\nfact of his being in love with the girl were a reason for doing his\r\nduty, it was at least not a reason for being rude. He had not yet come\r\nto that--to accepting rudeness as an incident of virtue; it had always\r\nbeen his theory that virtue had the best manners in the world, and he\r\nflattered himself at any rate that he could guard his integrity without\r\nmaking himself ridiculous. So, at what he thought a proper hour, in\r\nthe course of the morning, he retraced his steps along the little lane\r\nthrough which, two days ago, Angela Vivian had shown him the way to\r\nher mother\xe2\x80\x99s door. At this humble portal he knocked; the windows of the\r\nlittle chalet were open, and the white curtains, behind the flower-pots,\r\nwere fluttering as he had seen them before. The door was opened by\r\na neat young woman, who informed him very promptly that Madame and\r\nMademoiselle had left Blanquais a couple of hours earlier. They had gone\r\nto Paris--yes, very suddenly, taking with them but little luggage, and\r\nthey had left her--she had the honor of being the femme de chambre of\r\nces dames--to put up their remaining possessions and follow as soon\r\nas possible. On Bernard\xe2\x80\x99s expressing surprise and saying that he had\r\nsupposed them to be fixed at the sea-side for the rest of the season,\r\nthe femme de chambre, who seemed a very intelligent person, begged to\r\nremind him that the season was drawing to a close, that Madame had taken\r\nthe chalet but for five weeks, only ten days of which period were yet to\r\nexpire, that ces dames, as Monsieur perhaps knew, were great travellers,\r\nwho had been half over the world and thought nothing of breaking camp\r\nat an hour\xe2\x80\x99s notice, and that, in fine, Madame might very well have\r\nreceived a telegram summoning her to another part of the country.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd where have the ladies gone?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Bernard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor the moment, to Paris.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd in Paris where have they gone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDame, chez elles--to their house,\xe2\x80\x9d said the femme de chambre, who\r\nappeared to think that Bernard asked too many questions.\r\n\r\nBut Bernard persisted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is their house?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe waiting-maid looked at him from head to foot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf Monsieur wishes to write, many of Madame\xe2\x80\x99s letters come to her\r\nbanker,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, inscrutably.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd who is her banker?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe lives in the Rue de Provence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good--I will find him out,\xe2\x80\x9d said our hero, turning away.\r\n\r\nThe discriminating reader who has been so good as to interest himself\r\nin this little narrative will perhaps at this point exclaim with a\r\npardonable consciousness of shrewdness: \xe2\x80\x9cOf course he went the next day\r\nto the Rue de Provence!\xe2\x80\x9d Of course, yes; only as it happens Bernard did\r\nnothing of the kind. He did one of the most singular things he ever did\r\nin his life--a thing that puzzled him even at the time, and with regard\r\nto which he often afterward wondered whence he had drawn the ability\r\nfor so remarkable a feat--he simply spent a fortnight at\r\nBlanquais-les-Galets. It was a very quiet fortnight; he spoke to no one,\r\nhe formed no relations, he was company to himself. It may be added that\r\nhe had never found his own company half so good. He struck himself as\r\na reasonable, delicate fellow, who looked at things in such a way as\r\nto make him refrain--refrain successfully, that was the point--from\r\nconcerning himself practically about Angela Vivian. His saying that\r\nhe would find out the banker in the Rue de Provence had been for the\r\nbenefit of the femme de chambre, whom he thought rather impertinent; he\r\nhad really no intention whatever of entering that classic thoroughfare.\r\nHe took long walks, rambled on the beach, along the base of the cliffs\r\nand among the brown sea-caves, and he thought a good deal of certain\r\nincidents which have figured at an earlier stage of this narrative. He\r\nhad forbidden himself the future, as an object of contemplation, and\r\nit was therefore a matter of necessity that his imagination should take\r\nrefuge among the warm and familiar episodes of the past. He wondered why\r\nMrs. Vivian should have left the place so suddenly, and was of course\r\nstruck with the analogy between this incident and her abrupt departure\r\nfrom Baden. It annoyed him, it troubled him, but it by no means\r\nrekindled the alarm he had felt on first perceiving the injured Angela\r\non the beach. That alarm had been quenched by Angela\xe2\x80\x99s manner during\r\nthe hour that followed and during their short talk in the evening. This\r\nevening was to be forever memorable, for it had brought with it the\r\nrevelation which still, at moments, suddenly made Bernard tremble; but\r\nit had also brought him the assurance that Angela cared as little as\r\npossible for anything that a chance acquaintance might have said about\r\nher. It is all the more singular, therefore, that one evening, after he\r\nhad been at Blanquais a fortnight, a train of thought should suddenly\r\nhave been set in motion in his mind. It was kindled by no outward\r\noccurrence, but by some wandering spark of fancy or of memory, and the\r\nimmediate effect of it was to startle our hero very much as he had been\r\nstartled on the evening I have described. The circumstances were the\r\nsame; he had wandered down to the beach alone, very late, and he stood\r\nlooking at the duskily-tumbling sea. Suddenly the same voice that had\r\nspoken before murmured another phrase in the darkness, and it rang upon\r\nhis ear for the rest of the night. It startled him, as I have said,\r\nat first; then, the next morning, it led him to take his departure for\r\nParis. During the journey it lingered in his ear; he sat in the corner\r\nof the railway-carriage with his eyes closed, abstracted, on purpose to\r\nprolong the reverberation. If it were not true it was at least, as the\r\nItalians have it, ben trovato, and it was wonderful how well it bore\r\nthinking of. It bears telling less well; but I can at least give a hint\r\nof it. The theory that Angela hated him had evaporated in her presence,\r\nand another of a very different sort had sprung into being. It fitted\r\na great many of the facts, it explained a great many contradictions,\r\nanomalies, mysteries, and it accounted for Miss Vivian\xe2\x80\x99s insisting upon\r\nher mother\xe2\x80\x99s leaving Blanquais at a few hours\xe2\x80\x99 notice, even better\r\nthan the theory of her resentment could have done. At any rate, it\r\nobliterated Bernard\xe2\x80\x99s scruples very effectually, and led him on his\r\narrival in Paris to repair instantly to the Rue de Provence. This street\r\ncontains more than one banker, but there is one with whom Bernard deemed\r\nMrs. Vivian most likely to have dealings. He found he had reckoned\r\nrightly, and he had no difficulty in procuring her address. Having done\r\nso, however, he by no means went immediately to see her; he waited a\r\ncouple of days--perhaps to give those obliterated scruples I have spoken\r\nof a chance to revive. They kept very quiet, and it must be confessed\r\nthat Bernard took no great pains to recall them to life. After he had\r\nbeen in Paris three days, he knocked at Mrs. Vivian\xe2\x80\x99s door.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXII\r\n\r\nIt was opened by the little waiting-maid whom he had seen at Blanquais,\r\nand who looked at him very hard before she answered his inquiry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see I have found Mrs. Vivian\xe2\x80\x99s dwelling, though you would n\xe2\x80\x99t give\r\nme the address,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard said to her, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur has put some time to it!\xe2\x80\x9d the young woman answered dryly. And\r\nshe informed him that Madame was at home, though Mademoiselle, for whom\r\nhe had not asked, was not.\r\n\r\nMrs. Vivian occupied a diminutive apartment at the summit of one of\r\nthe tall white houses which ornament the neighborhood of the Arc de\r\nTriomphe. The early days of September had arrived, but Paris was still\r\na city of absentees. The weather was warm and charming, and a certain\r\nsavour of early autumn in the air was in accord with the somewhat\r\nmelancholy aspect of the empty streets and closed shutters of this\r\nhonorable quarter, where the end of the monumental vistas seemed to\r\nbe curtained with a hazy emanation from the Seine. It was late in\r\nthe afternoon when Bernard was ushered into Mrs. Vivian\xe2\x80\x99s little\r\nhigh-nestling drawing-room, and a patch of sunset tints, faintly red,\r\nrested softly upon the gilded wall. Bernard had seen these ladies only\r\nin borrowed and provisional abodes; but here was a place where they were\r\nreally living and which was stamped with their tastes, their habits,\r\ntheir charm. The little salon was very elegant; it contained a\r\nmultitude of pretty things, and it appeared to Bernard to be arranged\r\nin perfection. The long windows--the ceiling being low, they were really\r\nvery short--opened upon one of those solid balconies, occupying the\r\nwidth of the apartment, which are often in Paris a compensation for\r\nliving up five flights of stairs, and this balcony was filled with\r\nflowers and cushions. Bernard stepped out upon it to await the coming\r\nof Mrs. Vivian, and, as she was not quick to appear, he had time to\r\nsee that his friends enjoyed a magnificent view. They looked up at the\r\ntriumphal Arch, which presented itself at a picturesque angle, and near\r\nthe green tree-tops of the Champs Elysees, beyond which they caught a\r\nbroad gleam of the Seine and a glimpse, blue in the distance, of the\r\ngreat towers of Notre Dame. The whole vast city lay before them and\r\nbeneath them, with its ordered brilliancy and its mingled aspect of\r\ncompression and expansion; and yet the huge Parisian murmur died away\r\nbefore it reached Mrs. Vivian\xe2\x80\x99s sky-parlor, which seemed to Bernard the\r\nbrightest and quietest little habitation he had ever known.\r\n\r\nHis hostess came rustling in at last; she seemed agitated; she knocked\r\nover with the skirt of her dress a little gilded chair which was\r\nreflected in the polished parquet as in a sheet of looking-glass. Mrs.\r\nVivian had a fixed smile--she hardly knew what to say.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI found your address at the banker\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard. \xe2\x80\x9cYour maid, at\r\nBlanquais, refused to give it to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Vivian gave him a little look--there was always more or less of\r\nit in her face--which seemed equivalent to an entreaty that her\r\ninterlocutor should spare her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaids are so strange,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured; \xe2\x80\x9cespecially the French!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt pleased Bernard for the moment not to spare her, though he felt a\r\nsort of delight of kindness for her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour going off from Blanquais so suddenly, without leaving me any\r\nexplanation, any clue, any message of any sort--made me feel at first as\r\nif you did n\xe2\x80\x99t wish that I should look you up. It reminded me of the way\r\nyou left Baden--do you remember?--three years ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBaden was so charming--but one could n\xe2\x80\x99t stay forever,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs.\r\nVivian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had a sort of theory one could. Our life was so pleasant that it\r\nseemed a shame to break the spell, and if no one had moved I am sure we\r\nmight be sitting there now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Vivian stared, still with her little fixed smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think we should have had bad weather.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery likely,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cNature would have grown jealous\r\nof our good-humor--of our tranquil happiness. And after all, here we are\r\ntogether again--that is, some of us. But I have only my own audacity\r\nto thank for it. I was quite free to believe that you were not at all\r\npleased to see me re-appear--and it is only because I am not easy to\r\ndiscourage--am indeed probably a rather impudent fellow--that I have\r\nventured to come here to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very glad to see you re-appear, Mr. Longueville,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Vivian\r\ndeclared with the accent of veracity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was your daughter\xe2\x80\x99s idea, then, running away from Blanquais?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Vivian lowered her eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe were obliged to go to Fontainebleau. We have but just come back. I\r\nthought of writing to you,\xe2\x80\x9d she softly added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, what pleasure that would have given me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean, to tell you where we were, and that we should have been so\r\nhappy to see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thank you for the intention. I suppose your daughter would n\xe2\x80\x99t let\r\nyou carry it out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAngela is so peculiar,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Vivian said, simply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou told me that the first time I saw you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, at Siena,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Vivian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad to hear you speak frankly of that place!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps it \xe2\x80\x98s better,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Vivian murmured. She got up and went to the\r\nwindow; then stepping upon the balcony, she looked down a moment into\r\nthe street. \xe2\x80\x9cShe will come back in a moment,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, coming into the\r\nroom again. \xe2\x80\x9cShe has gone to see a friend who lives just beside us. We\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t mind about Siena now,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, softly.\r\n\r\nBernard understood her--understood this to be a retraction of the\r\nrequest she had made of him at Baden.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear little woman,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cshe wants to marry her\r\ndaughter still--only now she wants to marry her to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe wished to show her that he understood her, and he was on the point of\r\nseizing her hand, to do he did n\xe2\x80\x99t know what--to hold it, to press it,\r\nto kiss it--when he heard the sharp twang of the bell at the door of the\r\nlittle apartment.\r\n\r\nMrs. Vivian fluttered away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt \xe2\x80\x98s Angela,\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, and she stood there waiting and listening,\r\nsmiling at Bernard, with her handkerchief pressed to her lips.\r\n\r\nIn a moment the girl came into the drawing-room, but on seeing Bernard\r\nshe stopped, with her hand on the door-knob. Her mother went to her and\r\nkissed her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt \xe2\x80\x98s Mr. Longueville, dearest--he has found us out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFound us out?\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Angela, with a little laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a singular\r\nexpression!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was blushing as she had blushed when she first saw him at\r\nBlanquais. She seemed to Bernard now to have a great and peculiar\r\nbrightness--something she had never had before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI certainly have been looking for you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI was greatly\r\ndisappointed when I found you had taken flight from Blanquais.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTaken flight?\xe2\x80\x9d She repeated his words as she had repeated her mother\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is also a strange way of speaking!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care what I say,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, \xe2\x80\x9cso long as I make you\r\nunderstand that I have wanted very much to see you again, and that I\r\nhave wondered every day whether I might venture--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know why you should n\xe2\x80\x99t venture!\xe2\x80\x9d she interrupted, giving her\r\nlittle laugh again. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are not so terrible, are we, mamma?--that is,\r\nwhen once you have climbed our five flights of stairs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI came up very fast,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, \xe2\x80\x9cand I find your apartment\r\nmagnificent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Longueville must come again, must he not, dear?\xe2\x80\x9d asked mamma.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall come very often, with your leave,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard declared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will be immensely kind,\xe2\x80\x9d said Angela, looking away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not sure that you will think it that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you are trying to prove,\xe2\x80\x9d said Angela; \xe2\x80\x9cfirst that we\r\nran away from you, and then that we are not nice to our visitors.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, not that!\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard exclaimed; \xe2\x80\x9cfor I assure you I shall not care\r\nhow cold you are with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe walked away toward another door, which was masked with a curtain\r\nthat she lifted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad to hear that, for it gives me courage to say that I am very\r\ntired, and that I beg you will excuse me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe glanced at him a moment over her shoulder; then she passed out,\r\ndropping the curtain.\r\n\r\nBernard stood there face to face with Mrs. Vivian, whose eyes seemed to\r\nplead with him more than ever. In his own there was an excited smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease don\xe2\x80\x99t mind that,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cI know it \xe2\x80\x98s true that she is\r\ntired.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMind it, dear lady?\xe2\x80\x9d cried the young man. \xe2\x80\x9cI delight in it. It \xe2\x80\x98s just\r\nwhat I like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, she \xe2\x80\x98s very peculiar!\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Mrs. Vivian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is strange--yes. But I think I understand her a little.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must come back to-morrow, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope to have many to-morrows!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bernard as he took his\r\ndeparture.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIII\r\n\r\nAnd he had them in fact. He called the next day at the same hour, and he\r\nfound the mother and the daughter together in their pretty salon. Angela\r\nwas very gentle and gracious; he suspected Mrs. Vivian had given her a\r\ntender little lecture upon the manner in which she had received him the\r\nday before. After he had been there five minutes, Mrs. Vivian took a\r\ndecanter of water that was standing upon a table and went out on the\r\nbalcony to irrigate her flowers. Bernard watched her a while from his\r\nplace in the room; then she moved along the balcony and out of sight.\r\nSome ten minutes elapsed without her re-appearing, and then Bernard\r\nstepped to the threshold of the window and looked for her. She was not\r\nthere, and as he came and took his seat near Angela again, he announced,\r\nrather formally, that Mrs. Vivian had passed back into one of the other\r\nwindows.\r\n\r\nAngela was silent a moment--then she said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShould you like me to call her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was very peculiar--that was very true; yet Bernard held to his\r\ndeclaration of the day before that he now understood her a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I don\xe2\x80\x99t desire it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish to see you alone; I have\r\nsomething particular to say to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe turned her face toward him, and there was something in its\r\nexpression that showed him that he looked to her more serious than he\r\nhad ever looked. He sat down again; for some moments he hesitated to go\r\non.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou frighten me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said laughing; and in spite of her laugh this was\r\nobviously true.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI assure you my state of mind is anything but formidable. I am afraid\r\nof you, on the contrary; I am humble and apologetic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry for that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Angela. \xe2\x80\x9cI particularly dislike receiving\r\napologies, even when I know what they are for. What yours are for, I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t imagine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t dislike me--you don\xe2\x80\x99t hate me?\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard suddenly broke out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t ask me that humbly. Excuse me therefore if I say I have\r\nother, and more practical, things to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou despise me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is not humble either, for you seem to insist upon it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be after all a way of thinking of me, and I have a reason for\r\nwishing you to do that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI remember very well that you used to have a reason for everything. It\r\nwas not always a good one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis one is excellent,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, gravely. \xe2\x80\x9cI have been in love\r\nwith you for three years.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe got up slowly, turning away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that what you wished to say to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe went toward the open window, and he followed her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope it does n\xe2\x80\x99t offend you. I don\xe2\x80\x99t say it lightly--it \xe2\x80\x98s not a\r\npiece of gallantry. It \xe2\x80\x98s the very truth of my being. I did n\xe2\x80\x99t know it\r\ntill lately--strange as that may seem. I loved you long before I knew\r\nit--before I ventured or presumed to know it. I was thinking of you\r\nwhen I seemed to myself to be thinking of other things. It is very\r\nstrange--there are things in it I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand. I travelled over\r\nthe world, I tried to interest, to divert myself; but at bottom it was\r\na perfect failure. To see you again--that was what I wanted. When I saw\r\nyou last month at Blanquais I knew it; then everything became clear. It\r\nwas the answer to the riddle. I wished to read it very clearly--I wished\r\nto be sure; therefore I did n\xe2\x80\x99t follow you immediately. I questioned my\r\nheart--I cross-questioned it. It has borne the examination, and now I am\r\nsure. I am very sure. I love you as my life--I beg you to listen to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had listened--she had listened intently, looking straight out of the\r\nwindow and without moving.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have seen very little of me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, presently, turning her\r\nilluminated eye on him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have seen enough,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard added, smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must remember that at\r\nBaden I saw a good deal of you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but that did n\xe2\x80\x99t make you like me. I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard stood there a moment, frowning, with his eyes lowered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can imagine that. But I think I can explain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t explain now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Angela. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have said enough; explain some\r\nother time.\xe2\x80\x9d And she went out on the balcony.\r\n\r\nBernard, of course, in a moment was beside her, and, disregarding her\r\ninjunction, he began to explain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought I disliked you--but I have come to the conclusion it was just\r\nthe contrary. In reality I was in love with you. I had been so from the\r\nfirst time I saw you--when I made that sketch of you at Siena.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat in itself needs an explanation. I was not at all nice then--I was\r\nvery rude, very perverse. I was horrid!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you admit it!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bernard, with a sort of quick elation.\r\n\r\nShe had been pale, but she suddenly blushed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour own conduct was singular, as I remember it. It was not exactly\r\nagreeable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps not; but at least it was meant to be. I did n\xe2\x80\x99t know how to\r\nplease you then, and I am far from supposing that I have learned now.\r\nBut I entreat you to give me a chance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was silent a while; her eyes wandered over the great prospect of\r\nParis.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know how you can please me now?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, at last. \xe2\x80\x9cBy leaving\r\nme alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard looked at her a moment, then came straight back into the\r\ndrawing-room and took his hat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see I avail myself of the first chance. But I shall come back\r\nto-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am greatly obliged to you for what you have said. Such a speech as\r\nthat deserves to be listened to with consideration. You may come back\r\nto-morrow,\xe2\x80\x9d Angela added.\r\n\r\nOn the morrow, when he came back, she received him alone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did you know, at Baden, that I did n\xe2\x80\x99t like you?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, as soon\r\nas she would allow him.\r\n\r\nShe smiled, very gently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou assured me yesterday that you did like me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean that I supposed I did n\xe2\x80\x99t. How did you know that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can only say that I observed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must have observed very closely, for, superficially, I rather had\r\nthe air of admiring you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was very superficial.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t mean that; for, after all, that is just what my admiration,\r\nmy interest in you, were not. They were deep, they were latent. They\r\nwere not superficial--they were subterranean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are contradicting yourself, and I am perfectly consistent,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Angela. \xe2\x80\x9cYour sentiments were so well hidden that I supposed I\r\ndispleased you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI remember that at Baden, you used to contradict yourself,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard\r\nanswered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have a terrible memory!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t call it terrible, for it sees everything now in a charming\r\nlight--in the light of this understanding that we have at last arrived\r\nat, which seems to shine backward--to shine full on those Baden days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave we at last arrived at an understanding?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, with a grave\r\ndirectness which Bernard thought the most beautiful thing he had ever\r\nseen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt only depends upon you,\xe2\x80\x9d he declared; and then he broke out again\r\ninto a protestation of passionate tenderness. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t put me off this\r\ntime,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have had time to think about it; you have had\r\ntime to get over the surprise, the shock. I love you, and I offer you\r\neverything that belongs to me in this world.\xe2\x80\x9d As she looked at him with\r\nher dark, clear eyes, weighing this precious vow and yet not committing\r\nherself--\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you don\xe2\x80\x99t forgive me!\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured.\r\n\r\nShe gazed at him with the same solemn brightness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat have I to forgive you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis question seemed to him enchanting. He reached forward and took her\r\nhands, and if Mrs. Vivian had come in she would have seen him kneeling\r\nat her daughter\xe2\x80\x99s feet.\r\n\r\nBut Mrs. Vivian remained in seclusion, and Bernard saw her only the next\r\ntime he came.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very happy, because I think my daughter is happy,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what do you think of me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you are very clever. You must promise me to be very good to\r\nher.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am clever enough to promise that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you are good enough to keep it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Vivian. She looked\r\nas happy as she said, and her happiness gave her a communicative,\r\nconfidential tendency. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is very strange how things come about--how\r\nthe wheel turns round,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on. \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose there is no harm in my\r\ntelling you that I believe she always cared for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did n\xe2\x80\x99t you tell me before?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, with almost filial\r\nreproachfulness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow could I? I don\xe2\x80\x99t go about the world offering my daughter to\r\npeople--especially to indifferent people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt Baden you did n\xe2\x80\x99t think I was indifferent. You were afraid of my not\r\nbeing indifferent enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Vivian colored.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, at Baden I was a little too anxious!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo anxious I should n\xe2\x80\x99t speak to your daughter!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard,\r\nlaughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt Baden,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Vivian went on, \xe2\x80\x9cI had views. But I have n\xe2\x80\x99t any now--I\r\nhave given them up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat makes your acceptance of me very flattering!\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard exclaimed,\r\nlaughing still more gaily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have something better,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Vivian, laying her finger-tips on\r\nhis arm. \xe2\x80\x9cI have confidence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard did his best to encourage this gracious sentiment, and it seemed\r\nto him that there was something yet to be done to implant it more firmly\r\nin Angela\xe2\x80\x99s breast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have a confession to make to you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to her one day. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish\r\nyou would listen to it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it something very horrible?\xe2\x80\x9d Angela asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomething very horrible indeed. I once did you an injury.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn injury?\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated, in a tone which seemed to reduce the offence\r\nto contemptible proportions by simple vagueness of mind about it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what to call it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard. \xe2\x80\x9cA poor service--an\r\nill-turn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAngela gave a shrug, or rather an imitation of a shrug; for she was not\r\na shrugging person.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never knew it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI misrepresented you to Gordon Wright,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard went on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you speak to me of him?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked rather sadly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes it displease you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe hesitated a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it displeases me. If your confession has anything to do with him,\r\nI would rather not hear it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard returned to the subject another time--he had plenty of\r\nopportunities. He spent a portion of every day in the company of these\r\ndear women; and these days were the happiest of his life. The autumn\r\nweather was warm and soothing, the quartier was still deserted, and the\r\nuproar of the great city, which seemed a hundred miles away, reached\r\nthem through the dense October air with a softened and muffled sound.\r\nThe evenings, however, were growing cool, and before long they lighted\r\nthe first fire of the season in Mrs. Vivian\xe2\x80\x99s heavily draped little\r\nchimney-piece. On this occasion Bernard sat there with Angela, watching\r\nthe bright crackle of the wood and feeling that the charm of winter\r\nnights had begun. These two young persons were alone together in the\r\ngathering dusk; it was the hour before dinner, before the lamp had been\r\nlighted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI insist upon making you my confession,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall be very\r\nunhappy until you let me do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnhappy? You are the happiest of men.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI lie upon roses, if you will; but this memory, this remorse, is\r\na folded rose-leaf. I was completely mistaken about you at Baden; I\r\nthought all manner of evil of you--or at least I said it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMen are dull creatures,\xe2\x80\x9d said Angela.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think they are. So much so that, as I look back upon that time, there\r\nare some things I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand even now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see why you should look back. People in our position are\r\nsupposed to look forward.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t like those Baden days yourself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard. \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlike to think of them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a wonderful discovery!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard looked at her a moment in the brightening fire-light.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat part was it you tried to play there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAngela shook her head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMen are dull creatures.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have already granted that, and I am eating humble pie in asking for\r\nan explanation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you say of me?\xe2\x80\x9d Angela asked, after a silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said you were a coquette. Remember that I am simply historical.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe got up and stood in front of the fire, having her hand on the\r\nchimney-piece and looking down at the blaze. For some moments she\r\nremained there. Bernard could not see her face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said you were a dangerous woman to marry,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on deliberately.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said it because I thought it. I gave Gordon an opinion about you--it\r\nwas a very unfavorable one. I could n\xe2\x80\x99t make you out--I thought you were\r\nplaying a double part. I believed that you were ready to marry him, and\r\nyet I saw--I thought I saw--\xe2\x80\x9d and Bernard paused again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you see?\xe2\x80\x9d and Angela turned toward him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat you were encouraging me--playing with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you did n\xe2\x80\x99t like that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI liked it immensely--for myself! But did n\xe2\x80\x99t like it for Gordon; and\r\nI must do myself the justice to say that I thought more of him than of\r\nmyself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were an excellent friend,\xe2\x80\x9d said Angela, simply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe I was. And I am so still,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard added.\r\n\r\nShe shook her head sadly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor Mr. Wright!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is a dear good fellow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThoroughly good, and dear, doubtless to his wife, the affectionate\r\nBlanche.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t like him--you don\xe2\x80\x99t like her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose are two very different matters. I am very sorry for Mr. Wright.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou need n\xe2\x80\x99t be that. He is doing very well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you have already informed me. But I am sorry for him, all the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat does n\xe2\x80\x99t answer my question,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard exclaimed, with a certain\r\nirritation. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat part were you playing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat part do you think?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave n\xe2\x80\x99t I told you I gave it up, long ago?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAngela stood with her back to the fire, looking at him; her hands were\r\nlocked behind her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid it ever strike you that my position at Baden was a charming\r\none?--knowing that I had been handed over to you to be put under the\r\nmicroscope--like an insect with a pin stuck through it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow in the world did you know it? I thought we were particularly\r\ncareful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can a woman help knowing such a thing? She guesses it--she\r\ndiscovers it by instinct; especially if she be a proud woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, \xe2\x80\x9cif pride is a source of information, you must be a\r\nprodigy of knowledge!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know that you are particularly humble!\xe2\x80\x9d the girl retorted. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nmeekest and most submissive of her sex would not have consented to have\r\nsuch a bargain as that made about her--such a trick played upon her!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dearest Angela, it was no bargain--no trick!\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard interposed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was a clumsy trick--it was a bad bargain!\xe2\x80\x9d she declared. \xe2\x80\x9cAt any\r\nrate I hated it--I hated the idea of your pretending to pass judgment\r\nupon me; of your having come to Baden for the purpose. It was as if Mr.\r\nWright had been buying a horse and you had undertaken to put me through\r\nmy paces!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI undertook nothing--I declined to undertake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou certainly made a study of me--and I was determined you should get\r\nyour lesson wrong. I determined to embarrass, to mislead, to defeat you.\r\nOr rather, I did n\xe2\x80\x99t determine; I simply obeyed a natural impulse of\r\nself-defence--the impulse to evade the fierce light of criticism. I\r\nwished to put you in the wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou did it all very well. You put me admirably in the wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe only justification for my doing it at all was my doing it well,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Angela.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were justified then! You must have hated me fiercely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe turned her back to him and stood looking at the fire again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, there are some things that I did that can be accounted for only by\r\nan intense aversion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe said this so naturally that in spite of a certain theory that was\r\ntouched upon a few pages back, Bernard was a good deal bewildered. He\r\nrose from the sofa where he had been lounging and went and stood beside\r\nher a moment. Then he passed his arm round her waist and murmured an\r\nalmost timorous--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you are trying to make me say!\xe2\x80\x9d she answered.\r\n\r\nHe looked down at her for a moment as he held her close to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see, after all, why I should wish to make you say it. It would\r\nonly make my remorse more acute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was musing, with her eyes on the fire, and for a moment she made no\r\nanswer; then, as if her attention were returning--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you still talking about your remorse?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see I put it very strongly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat I was a horrid creature?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat you were not a woman to marry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, my poor Bernard,\xe2\x80\x9d said Angela, \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t attempt to prove to you\r\nthat you are not inconsistent!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe month of September drew to a close, and she consented to fix a day\r\nfor their wedding. The last of October was the moment selected, and the\r\nselection was almost all that was wanting to Bernard\xe2\x80\x99s happiness. I say\r\n\xe2\x80\x9calmost,\xe2\x80\x9d for there was a solitary spot in his consciousness which felt\r\nnumb and dead--unpervaded by the joy with which the rest of his spirit\r\nseemed to thrill and tingle. The removal of this hard grain in the sweet\r\nsavour of life was needed to complete his felicity. Bernard felt that he\r\nhad made the necessary excision when, at the end of the month, he\r\nwrote to Gordon Wright of his engagement. He had been putting off\r\nthe performance of this duty from day to day--it seemed so hard to\r\naccomplish it gracefully. He did it at the end very briefly; it struck\r\nhim that this was the best way. Three days after he had sent his letter\r\nthere arrived one from Gordon himself, informing Bernard that he had\r\nsuddenly determined to bring Blanche to Europe. She was not well, and\r\nthey would lose no time. They were to sail within a week after his\r\nwriting. The letter contained a postscript--\xe2\x80\x9cCaptain Lovelock comes with\r\nus.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIV\r\n\r\nBernard prepared for Gordon\xe2\x80\x99s arrival in Paris, which, according to his\r\nletter, would take place in a few days. He was not intending to stop in\r\nEngland; Blanche desired to proceed immediately to the French capital,\r\nto confer with her man-milliner, after which it was probable that they\r\nwould go to Italy or to the East for the winter. \xe2\x80\x9cI have given her a\r\nchoice of Rome or the Nile,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gordon, \xe2\x80\x9cbut she tells me she does n\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncare a fig where we go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI say that Bernard prepared to receive his friends, and I mean that he\r\nprepared morally--or even intellectually. Materially speaking, he could\r\nsimply hold himself in readiness to engage an apartment at a hotel and\r\nto go to meet them at the station. He expected to hear from Gordon\r\nas soon as this interesting trio should reach England, but the first\r\nnotification he received came from a Parisian hotel. It came to him in\r\nthe shape of a very short note, in the morning, shortly before lunch,\r\nand was to the effect that his friends had alighted in the Rue de la\r\nPaix the night before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe were tired, and I have slept late,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gordon; \xe2\x80\x9cotherwise you\r\nshould have heard from me earlier. Come to lunch, if possible. I want\r\nextremely to see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard, of course, made a point of going to lunch. In as short a time\r\nas possible he found himself in Gordon\xe2\x80\x99s sitting-room at the Hotel\r\nMiddlesex. The table was laid for the midday repast, and a gentleman\r\nstood with his back to the door, looking out of the window. As Bernard\r\ncame in, this gentleman turned and exhibited the ambrosial beard, the\r\nsymmetrical shape, the monocular appendage, of Captain Lovelock.\r\n\r\nThe Captain screwed his glass into his eye, and greeted Bernard in his\r\nusual fashion--that is, as if he had parted with him overnight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, good morning! Beastly morning, is n\xe2\x80\x99t it? I suppose you are come to\r\nluncheon--I have come to luncheon. It ought to be on table, you know--it\r\n\xe2\x80\x98s nearly two o\xe2\x80\x99clock. But I dare say you have noticed foreigners are\r\nnever punctual--it \xe2\x80\x98s only English servants that are punctual. And they\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t understand luncheon, you know--they can\xe2\x80\x99t make out our eating at\r\nthis sort of hour. You know they always dine so beastly early. Do you\r\nremember the sort of time they used to dine at Baden?--half-past five,\r\nhalf-past six; some unearthly hour of that kind. That \xe2\x80\x98s the sort of\r\ntime you dine in America. I found they \xe2\x80\x98d invite a man at half-past six.\r\nThat \xe2\x80\x98s what I call being in a hurry for your food. You know they always\r\naccuse the Americans of making a rush for their victuals. I am bound\r\nto say that in New York, and that sort of place, the victuals were very\r\ngood when you got them. I hope you don\xe2\x80\x99t mind my saying anything about\r\nAmerica? You know the Americans are so deucedly thin-skinned--they\r\nalways bristle up if you say anything against their institutions. The\r\nEnglish don\xe2\x80\x99t care a rap what you say--they \xe2\x80\x98ve got a different sort\r\nof temper, you know. With the Americans I \xe2\x80\x98m deuced careful--I never\r\nbreathe a word about anything. While I was over there I went in for\r\nbeing complimentary. I laid it on thick, and I found they would take all\r\nI could give them. I did n\xe2\x80\x99t see much of their institutions, after all;\r\nI went in for seeing the people. Some of the people were charming--upon\r\nmy soul, I was surprised at some of the people. I dare say you know some\r\nof the people I saw; they were as nice people as you would see anywhere.\r\nThere were always a lot of people about Mrs. Wright, you know; they\r\ntold me they were all the best people. You know she is always late for\r\neverything. She always comes in after every one is there--looking so\r\ndevilish pretty, pulling on her gloves. She wears the longest gloves I\r\never saw in my life. Upon my word, if they don\xe2\x80\x99t come, I think I will\r\nring the bell and ask the waiter what \xe2\x80\x98s the matter. Would n\xe2\x80\x99t you ring\r\nthe bell? It \xe2\x80\x98s a great mistake, their trying to carry out their ideas\r\nof lunching. That \xe2\x80\x98s Wright\xe2\x80\x99s character, you know; he \xe2\x80\x98s always trying\r\nto carry out some idea. When I am abroad, I go in for the foreign\r\nbreakfast myself. You may depend upon it they had better give up trying\r\nto do this sort of thing at this hour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCaptain Lovelock was more disposed to conversation than Bernard had\r\nknown him before. His discourse of old had been languid and fragmentary,\r\nand our hero had never heard him pursue a train of ideas through so\r\nmany involutions. To Bernard\xe2\x80\x99s observant eye, indeed, the Captain was\r\nan altered man. His manner betrayed a certain restless desire to be\r\nagreeable, to anticipate judgment--a disposition to smile, and be civil,\r\nand entertain his auditor, a tendency to move about and look out of the\r\nwindow and at the clock. He struck Bernard as a trifle nervous--as\r\nless solidly planted on his feet than when he lounged along the Baden\r\ngravel-walks by the side of his usual companion--a lady for whom,\r\napparently, his admiration was still considerable. Bernard was curious\r\nto see whether he would ring the bell to inquire into the delay\r\nattending the service of lunch; but before this sentiment, rather idle\r\nunder the circumstances, was gratified, Blanche passed into the room\r\nfrom a neighboring apartment. To Bernard\xe2\x80\x99s perception Blanche, at least,\r\nwas always Blanche; she was a person in whom it would not have occurred\r\nto him to expect any puzzling variation, and the tone of her little,\r\nsoft, thin voice instantly rang in his ear like an echo of yesterday\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntalk. He had already remarked to himself that after however long\r\nan interval one might see Blanche, she re-appeared with an air of\r\nfamiliarity. This was in some sense, indeed, a proof of the agreeable\r\nimpression she made, and she looked exceedingly pretty as she now\r\nsuddenly stopped on seeing our two gentlemen, and gave a little cry of\r\nsurprise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! I did n\xe2\x80\x99t know you were here. They never told me. Have you been\r\nwaiting a long time? How d\xe2\x80\x99 ye do? You must think we are polite.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n She held out her hand to Bernard, smiling very graciously. At Captain\r\nLovelock she barely glanced. \xe2\x80\x9cI hope you are very well,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on to\r\nLongueville; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I need n\xe2\x80\x99t ask that. You \xe2\x80\x98re as blooming as a rose.\r\nWhat in the world has happened to you? You look so brilliant--so fresh.\r\nCan you say that to a man--that he looks fresh? Or can you only say that\r\nabout butter and eggs?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt depends upon the man,\xe2\x80\x9d said Captain Lovelock. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t say that a\r\nman \xe2\x80\x98s fresh who spends his time in running about after you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, are you here?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Blanche with another little cry of surprise.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did n\xe2\x80\x99t notice you--I thought you were the waiter. This is what\r\nhe calls running about after me,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, to Bernard; \xe2\x80\x9ccoming to\r\nbreakfast without being asked. How queerly they have arranged the\r\ntable!\xe2\x80\x9d she went on, gazing with her little elevated eyebrows at this\r\npiece of furniture. \xe2\x80\x9cI always thought that in Paris, if they could\r\nn\xe2\x80\x99t do anything else, they could arrange a table. I don\xe2\x80\x99t like that\r\nat all--those horrid little dishes on each side! Don\xe2\x80\x99t you think those\r\nthings ought to be off the table, Mr. Longueville? I don\xe2\x80\x99t like to see\r\na lot of things I \xe2\x80\x98m not eating. And I told them to have some\r\nflowers--pray, where are the flowers? Do they call those things\r\nflowers? They look as if they had come out of the landlady\xe2\x80\x99s bonnet! Mr.\r\nLongueville, do look at those objects.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are not like me--they are not very fresh,\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Bernard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt \xe2\x80\x98s no great matter--we have not got to eat them,\xe2\x80\x9d growled Captain\r\nLovelock.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think you would expect to--with the luncheon you usually\r\nmake!\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Blanche. \xe2\x80\x9cSince you are here, though I did n\xe2\x80\x99t ask you,\r\nyou might as well make yourself useful. Will you be so good as to ring\r\nthe bell? If Gordon expects that we are going to wait another quarter of\r\nan hour for him he exaggerates the patience of a long-suffering wife. If\r\nyou are very curious to know what he is about, he is writing letters, by\r\nway of a change. He writes about eighty a day; his correspondents\r\nmust be strong people! It \xe2\x80\x98s a lucky thing for me that I am married\r\nto Gordon; if I were not he might write to me--to me, to whom it \xe2\x80\x98s a\r\nmisery to have to answer even an invitation to dinner! To begin with, I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know how to spell. If Captain Lovelock ever boasts that he has\r\nhad letters from me, you may know it \xe2\x80\x98s an invention. He has never had\r\nanything but telegrams--three telegrams--that I sent him in America\r\nabout a pair of slippers that he had left at our house and that I did\r\nn\xe2\x80\x99t know what to do with. Captain Lovelock\xe2\x80\x99s slippers are no trifle\r\nto have on one\xe2\x80\x99s hands--on one\xe2\x80\x99s feet, I suppose I ought to say. For\r\ntelegrams the spelling does n\xe2\x80\x99t matter; the people at the office correct\r\nit--or if they don\xe2\x80\x99t you can put it off on them. I never see anything\r\nnowadays but Gordon\xe2\x80\x99s back,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on, as they took their places at\r\ntable--\xe2\x80\x9chis noble broad back, as he sits writing his letters. That \xe2\x80\x98s my\r\nprincipal view of my husband. I think that now we are in Paris I ought\r\nto have a portrait of it by one of the great artists. It would be such a\r\ncharacteristic pose. I have quite forgotten his face and I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I\r\nshould know it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGordon\xe2\x80\x99s face, however, presented itself just at this moment; he came in\r\nquickly, with his countenance flushed with the pleasure of meeting his\r\nold friend again. He had the sun-scorched look of a traveller who has\r\njust crossed the Atlantic, and he smiled at Bernard with his honest\r\neyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t think me a great brute for not being here to receive you,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid, as he clasped his hand. \xe2\x80\x9cI was writing an important letter and I\r\nput it to myself in this way: \xe2\x80\x98If I interrupt my letter I shall have to\r\ncome back and finish it; whereas if I finish it now, I can have all the\r\nrest of the day to spend with him.\xe2\x80\x99 So I stuck to it to the end, and now\r\nwe can be inseparable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may be sure Gordon reasoned it out,\xe2\x80\x9d said Blanche, while her\r\nhusband offered his hand in silence to Captain Lovelock.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGordon\xe2\x80\x99s reasoning is as fine as other people\xe2\x80\x99s feeling!\xe2\x80\x9d declared\r\nBernard, who was conscious of a desire to say something very pleasant to\r\nGordon, and who did not at all approve of Blanche\xe2\x80\x99s little ironical tone\r\nabout her husband.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Bernard\xe2\x80\x99s compliments are better than either,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gordon,\r\nlaughing and taking his seat at table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been paying him compliments,\xe2\x80\x9d Blanche went on. \xe2\x80\x9cI have been\r\ntelling him he looks so brilliant, so blooming--as if something had\r\nhappened to him, as if he had inherited a fortune. He must have been\r\ndoing something very wicked, and he ought to tell us all about it,\r\nto amuse us. I am sure you are a dreadful Parisian, Mr. Longueville.\r\nRemember that we are three dull, virtuous people, exceedingly bored\r\nwith each other\xe2\x80\x99s society, and wanting to hear something strange and\r\nexciting. If it \xe2\x80\x98s a little improper, that won\xe2\x80\x99t spoil it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou certainly are looking uncommonly well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gordon, still smiling,\r\nacross the table, at his friend. \xe2\x80\x9cI see what Blanche means--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Gordon, that \xe2\x80\x98s a great event,\xe2\x80\x9d his wife interposed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt \xe2\x80\x98s a good deal to pretend, certainly,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, smiling always,\r\nwith his red face and his blue eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cBut this is no great credit to me,\r\nbecause Bernard\xe2\x80\x99s superb condition would strike any one. You look as if\r\nyou were going to marry the Lord Mayor\xe2\x80\x99s daughter!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIf Bernard was blooming, his bloom at this juncture must have deepened,\r\nand in so doing indeed have contributed an even brighter tint to his\r\nexpression of salubrious happiness. It was one of the rare occasions of\r\nhis life when he was at a loss for a verbal expedient.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt \xe2\x80\x98s a great match,\xe2\x80\x9d he nevertheless murmured, jestingly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must\r\nexcuse my inflated appearance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt has absorbed you so much that you have had no time to write to me,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Gordon. \xe2\x80\x9cI expected to hear from you after you arrived.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wrote to you a fortnight ago--just before receiving your own letter.\r\nYou left New York before my letter reached it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, it will have crossed us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gordon. \xe2\x80\x9cBut now that we have your\r\nsociety I don\xe2\x80\x99t care. Your letters, of course, are delightful, but that\r\nis still better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn spite of this sympathetic statement Bernard cannot be said to have\r\nenjoyed his lunch; he was thinking of something else that lay before him\r\nand that was not agreeable. He was like a man who has an acrobatic feat\r\nto perform--a wide ditch to leap, a high pole to climb--and who has a\r\npresentiment of fractures and bruises. Fortunately he was not obliged to\r\ntalk much, as Mrs. Gordon displayed even more than her usual vivacity,\r\nrendering her companions the graceful service of lifting the burden of\r\nconversation from their shoulders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you were surprised to see us rushing out here so suddenly,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n she observed in the course of the repast. \xe2\x80\x9cWe had said nothing about\r\nit when you last saw us, and I believe we are supposed to tell you\r\neverything, ain\xe2\x80\x99t we? I certainly have told you a great many things, and\r\nthere are some of them I hope you have n\xe2\x80\x99t repeated. I have no doubt\r\nyou have told them all over Paris, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t care what you tell in\r\nParis--Paris is n\xe2\x80\x99t so easily shocked. Captain Lovelock does n\xe2\x80\x99t repeat\r\nwhat I tell him; I set him up as a model of discretion. I have told him\r\nsome pretty bad things, and he has liked them so much he has kept them\r\nall to himself. I say my bad things to Captain Lovelock, and my good\r\nthings to other people; he does n\xe2\x80\x99t know the difference and he is\r\nperfectly content.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOther people as well often don\xe2\x80\x99t know the difference,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gordon,\r\ngravely. \xe2\x80\x9cYou ought always to tell us which are which.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBlanche gave her husband a little impertinent stare.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I am not appreciated,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with an attempt at superior\r\ndryness, \xe2\x80\x9cI am too proud to point it out. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know whether you know\r\nthat I \xe2\x80\x98m proud,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on, turning to Gordon and glancing at Captain\r\nLovelock; \xe2\x80\x9cit \xe2\x80\x98s a good thing to know. I suppose Gordon will say that I\r\nought to be too proud to point that out; but what are you to do when no\r\none has any imagination? You have a grain or two, Mr. Longueville; but\r\nCaptain Lovelock has n\xe2\x80\x99t a speck. As for Gordon, je n\xe2\x80\x99en parle pas! But\r\neven you, Mr. Longueville, would never imagine that I am an interesting\r\ninvalid--that we are travelling for my delicate health. The doctors have\r\nn\xe2\x80\x99t given me up, but I have given them up. I know I don\xe2\x80\x99t look as if I\r\nwere out of health; but that \xe2\x80\x98s because I always try to look my best.\r\nMy appearance proves nothing--absolutely nothing. Do you think my\r\nappearance proves anything, Captain Lovelock?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCaptain Lovelock scrutinized Blanche\xe2\x80\x99s appearance with a fixed and\r\nsolemn eye; and then he replied--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt proves you are very lovely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBlanche kissed her finger-tips to him in return for this compliment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou only need to give Captain Lovelock a chance,\xe2\x80\x9d she rattled on, \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\nhe is as clever as any one. That \xe2\x80\x98s what I like to do to my friends--I\r\nlike to make chances for them. Captain Lovelock is like my dear little\r\nblue terrier that I left at home. If I hold out a stick he will jump\r\nover it. He won\xe2\x80\x99t jump without the stick; but as soon as I produce it he\r\nknows what he has to do. He looks at it a moment and then he gives his\r\nlittle hop. He knows he will have a lump of sugar, and Captain Lovelock\r\nexpects one as well. Dear Captain Lovelock, shall I ring for a lump?\r\nWould n\xe2\x80\x99t it be touching? Garcon, un morceau de sucre pour Monsieur\r\nle Capitaine! But what I give Monsieur le Capitaine is moral sugar! I\r\nusually administer it in private, and he shall have a good big morsel\r\nwhen you go away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGordon got up, turning to Bernard and looking at his watch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us go away, in that case,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, smiling, \xe2\x80\x9cand leave Captain\r\nLovelock to receive his reward. We will go and take a walk; we will go\r\nup the Champs Elysees. Good morning, Monsieur le Capitaine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNeither Blanche nor the Captain offered any opposition to this proposal,\r\nand Bernard took leave of his hostess and joined Gordon, who had already\r\npassed into the antechamber.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXV\r\n\r\nGordon took his arm and they gained the street; they strolled in the\r\ndirection of the Champs Elysees.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor a little exercise and a good deal of talk, it \xe2\x80\x98s the pleasantest\r\nplace,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gordon. \xe2\x80\x9cI have a good deal to say; I have a good deal to\r\nask you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard felt the familiar pressure of his friend\xe2\x80\x99s hand, as it rested on\r\nhis arm, and it seemed to him never to have lain there with so heavy a\r\nweight. It held him fast--it held him to account; it seemed a physical\r\nsymbol of responsibility. Bernard was not re-assured by hearing that\r\nGordon had a great deal to say, and he expected a sudden explosion of\r\nbitterness on the subject of Blanche\xe2\x80\x99s irremediable triviality. The\r\nafternoon was a lovely one--the day was a perfect example of the\r\nmellowest mood of autumn. The air was warm and filled with a golden\r\nhaze, which seemed to hang about the bare Parisian trees, as if with a\r\ntender impulse to drape their nakedness. A fine day in Paris brings\r\nout a wonderfully bright and appreciative multitude of strollers and\r\nloungers, and the liberal spaces of the Champs Elysees were on this\r\noccasion filled with those placid votaries of inexpensive entertainment\r\nwho abound in the French capital. The benches and chairs on the edge of\r\nthe great avenue exhibited a dense fraternity of gazers, and up and down\r\nthe broad walk passed the slow-moving and easily pleased pedestrians.\r\nGordon, in spite of his announcement that he had a good deal to say,\r\nconfined himself at first to superficial allusions, and Bernard after a\r\nwhile had the satisfaction of perceiving that he was not likely, for the\r\nmoment, to strike the note of conjugal discord. He appeared, indeed, to\r\nfeel no desire to speak of Blanche in any manner whatever. He fell into\r\nthe humor of the hour and the scene, looked at the crowd, talked about\r\ntrifles. He remarked that Paris was a wonderful place after all, and\r\nthat a little glimpse of the Parisian picture was a capital thing as a\r\nchange; said he was very glad they had come, and that for his part he\r\nwas willing to stay three months.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what have you been doing with yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked. \xe2\x80\x9cHow have you\r\nbeen occupied, and what are you meaning to do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard said nothing for a moment, and Gordon presently glanced at\r\nhis face to see why he was silent. Bernard, looking askance, met his\r\ncompanion\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, and then, resting his own upon them, he stopped short.\r\nHis heart was beating; it was a question of saying to Gordon outright,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been occupied in becoming engaged to Angela Vivian.\xe2\x80\x9d But he\r\ncould n\xe2\x80\x99t say it, and yet he must say something. He tried to invent\r\nsomething; but he could think of nothing, and still Gordon was looking\r\nat him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am so glad to see you!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed, for want of something better;\r\nand he blushed--he felt foolish, he felt false--as he said it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Bernard!\xe2\x80\x9d Gordon murmured gratefully, as they walked on. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\n\xe2\x80\x98s very good of you to say that; I am very glad we are together again.\r\nI want to say something,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, in a moment; \xe2\x80\x9cI hope you won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmind it--\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard gave a little laugh at his companion\xe2\x80\x99s scruples, and\r\nGordon continued. \xe2\x80\x9cTo tell the truth, it has sometimes seemed to me that\r\nwe were not so good friends as we used to be--that something had come\r\nbetween us--I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know why. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what to\r\ncall it but a sort of lowering of the temperature. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know whether\r\nyou have felt it, or whether it has been simply a fancy of mine.\r\nWhatever it may have been, it \xe2\x80\x98s all over, is n\xe2\x80\x99t it? We are too old\r\nfriends--too good friends--not to stick together. Of course, the rubs of\r\nlife may occasionally loosen the cohesion; but it is very good to feel\r\nthat, with a little direct contact, it may easily be re-established.\r\nIs n\xe2\x80\x99t that so? But we should n\xe2\x80\x99t reason about these things; one feels\r\nthem, and that \xe2\x80\x98s enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGordon spoke in his clear, cheerful voice, and Bernard listened\r\nintently. It seemed to him there was an undertone of pain and effort in\r\nhis companion\xe2\x80\x99s speech; it was that of an unhappy man trying to be wise\r\nand make the best of things.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, the rubs of life--the rubs of life!\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard repeated vaguely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must n\xe2\x80\x99t mind them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gordon, with a conscientious laugh.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must toughen our hides; or, at the worst, we must plaster up our\r\nbruises. But why should we choose this particular place and hour for\r\ntalking of the pains of life?\xe2\x80\x9d he went on. \xe2\x80\x9cAre we not in the midst of\r\nits pleasures? I mean, henceforth, to cultivate its pleasures. What\r\nare yours, just now, Bernard? Is n\xe2\x80\x99t it supposed that in Paris one must\r\namuse one\xe2\x80\x99s self? How have you been amusing yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been leading a very quiet life,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI notice that \xe2\x80\x98s what people always say when they have been\r\nparticularly dissipated. What have you done? Whom have you seen that one\r\nknows?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard was silent a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have seen some old friends of yours,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last. \xe2\x80\x9cI have seen\r\nMrs. Vivian and her daughter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d Gordon made this exclamation, and then stopped short. Bernard\r\nlooked at him, but Gordon was looking away; his eyes had caught some one\r\nin the crowd. Bernard followed the direction they had taken, and then\r\nGordon went on: \xe2\x80\x9cTalk of the devil--excuse the adage! Are not those the\r\nladies in question?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Vivian and her daughter were, in fact, seated among a great many\r\nother quiet people, in a couple of hired chairs, at the edge of the\r\ngreat avenue. They were turned toward our two friends, and when Bernard\r\ndistinguished them, in the well-dressed multitude, they were looking\r\nstraight at Gordon Wright.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey see you!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou say that as if I wished to run away,\xe2\x80\x9d Gordon answered. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwant to run away; on the contrary, I want to speak to them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat \xe2\x80\x98s easily done,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, and they advanced to the two\r\nladies.\r\n\r\nMrs. Vivian and her daughter rose from their chairs as they came; they\r\nhad evidently rapidly exchanged observations, and had decided that\r\nit would facilitate their interview with Gordon Wright to receive him\r\nstanding. He made his way to them through the crowd, blushing deeply,\r\nas he always did when excited; then he stood there bare-headed,\r\nshaking hands with each of them, with a fixed smile, and with nothing,\r\napparently, to say. Bernard watched Angela\xe2\x80\x99s face; she was giving his\r\ncompanion a beautiful smile. Mrs. Vivian was delicately cordial.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was sure it was you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gordon at last. \xe2\x80\x9cWe were just talking of\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid Mr. Longueville deny it was we?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mrs. Vivian, archly; \xe2\x80\x9cafter\r\nwe had supposed that we had made an impression on him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew you were in Paris--we were in the act of talking of you,\xe2\x80\x9d Gordon\r\nwent on. \xe2\x80\x9cI am very glad to see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard had shaken hands with Angela, looking at her intently; and in\r\nher eyes, as his own met them, it seemed to him that there was a gleam\r\nof mockery. At whom was she mocking--at Gordon, or at himself? Bernard\r\nwas uncomfortable enough not to care to be mocked; but he felt even more\r\nsorry that Gordon should be.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe also knew you were coming--Mr. Longueville had told us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs.\r\nVivian; \xe2\x80\x9cand we have been expecting the pleasure of seeing Blanche. Dear\r\nlittle Blanche!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear little Blanche will immediately come and see you,\xe2\x80\x9d Gordon replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImmediately, we hope,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Vivian. \xe2\x80\x9cWe shall be so very glad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Bernard perceived that she wished to say something soothing and\r\nsympathetic to poor Gordon; having it, as he supposed, on her\r\nconscience that, after having once encouraged him to regard himself\r\nas indispensable (in the capacity of son-in-law) to her happiness,\r\nshe should now present to him the spectacle of a felicity which had\r\nestablished itself without his aid. \xe2\x80\x9cWe were so very much interested in\r\nyour marriage,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on. \xe2\x80\x9cWe thought it so--so delightful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGordon fixed his eyes on the ground for a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI owe it partly to you,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered. \xe2\x80\x9cYou had done so much for\r\nBlanche. You had so cultivated her mind and polished her manners that\r\nher attractions were doubled, and I fell an easy victim to them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe uttered these words with an exaggerated solemnity, the result of\r\nwhich was to produce, for a moment, an almost embarrassing silence.\r\nBernard was rapidly becoming more and more impatient of his own\r\nembarrassment, and now he exclaimed, in a loud and jovial voice--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlanche makes victims by the dozen! I was a victim last winter; we are\r\nall victims!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear little Blanche!\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Vivian murmured again.\r\n\r\nAngela had said nothing; she had simply stood there, making no attempt\r\nto address herself to Gordon, and yet with no affectation of reserve or\r\nof indifference. Now she seemed to feel the impulse to speak to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen Blanche comes to see us, you must be sure to come with her,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid, with a friendly smile.\r\n\r\nGordon looked at her, but he said nothing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe were so sorry to hear she is out of health,\xe2\x80\x9d Angela went on.\r\n\r\nStill Gordon was silent, with his eyes fixed on her expressive and\r\ncharming face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not serious,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe used to be so well--so bright,\xe2\x80\x9d said Angela, who also appeared to\r\nhave the desire to say something kind and comfortable.\r\n\r\nGordon made no response to this; he only looked at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope you are well, Miss Vivian,\xe2\x80\x9d he broke out at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, thank you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you live in Paris?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have pitched our tent here for the present.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you like it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI find it no worse than other places.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGordon appeared to desire to talk with her; but he could think of\r\nnothing to say. Talking with her was a pretext for looking at her;\r\nand Bernard, who thought she had never been so handsome as at that\r\nparticular moment, smiling at her troubled ex-lover, could easily\r\nconceive that his friend should desire to prolong this privilege.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you been sitting here long?\xe2\x80\x9d Gordon asked, thinking of something\r\nat last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHalf an hour. We came out to walk, and my mother felt tired. It is time\r\nwe should turn homeward,\xe2\x80\x9d Angela added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I am tired, my daughter. We must take a voiture, if Mr.\r\nLongueville will be so good as to find us one,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Vivian.\r\n\r\nBernard, professing great alacrity, looked about him; but he still\r\nlingered near his companions. Gordon had thought of something else.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you been to Baden again?\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard heard him ask. But at this\r\nmoment Bernard espied at a distance an empty hackney-carriage crawling\r\nup the avenue, and he was obliged to go and signal to it. When he came\r\nback, followed by the vehicle, the two ladies, accompanied by Gordon,\r\nhad come to the edge of the pavement. They shook hands with Gordon\r\nbefore getting into the cab, and Mrs. Vivian exclaimed--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe sure you give our love to your dear wife!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen the two ladies settled themselves and smiled their adieux, and the\r\nlittle victoria rumbled away at an easy pace, while Bernard stood with\r\nGordon, looking after it. They watched it a moment, and then Gordon\r\nturned to his companion. He looked at Bernard for some moments intently,\r\nwith a singular expression.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is strange for me to see her!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, presently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope it is not altogether disagreeable,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard answered smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is delightfully handsome,\xe2\x80\x9d Gordon went on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is a beautiful woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the strange thing is that she strikes me now so differently,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Gordon continued. \xe2\x80\x9cI used to think her so mysterious--so ambiguous. She\r\nseems to be now so simple.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, laughing, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s an improvement!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo simple and so good!\xe2\x80\x9d Gordon exclaimed.\r\n\r\nBernard laid his hand on his companion\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder, shaking his head\r\nslowly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must not think too much about that,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo simple--so good--so charming!\xe2\x80\x9d Gordon repeated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, my dear Gordon!\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard murmured.\r\n\r\nBut still Gordon continued.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo intelligent, so reasonable, so sensible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you discovered all that in two minutes\xe2\x80\x99 talk?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, in two minutes\xe2\x80\x99 talk. I should n\xe2\x80\x99t hesitate about her now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt \xe2\x80\x98s better you should n\xe2\x80\x99t say that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should n\xe2\x80\x99t I say it? It seems to me it \xe2\x80\x98s my duty to say it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo--your duty lies elsewhere,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are two reasons.\r\nOne is that you have married another woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat difference does that make?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Gordon.\r\n\r\nBernard made no attempt to answer this inquiry; he simply went on--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe other is--the other is--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut here he paused.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the other?\xe2\x80\x9d Gordon asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat I am engaged to marry Miss Vivian.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd with this Bernard took his hand off Gordon\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder.\r\n\r\nGordon stood staring.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo marry Miss Vivian?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow that Bernard had heard himself say it, audibly, distinctly, loudly,\r\nthe spell of his apprehension seemed broken, and he went on bravely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are to be married very shortly. It has all come about within a few\r\nweeks. It will seem to you very strange--perhaps you won\xe2\x80\x99t like it. That\r\n\xe2\x80\x98s why I have hesitated to tell you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGordon turned pale; it was the first time Bernard had ever seen him do\r\nso; evidently he did not like it. He stood staring and frowning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I thought--I thought,\xe2\x80\x9d he began at last--\xe2\x80\x9cI thought that you\r\ndisliked her!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI supposed so, too,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I have got over that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGordon turned away, looking up the great avenue into the crowd. Then\r\nturning back, he said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very much surprised.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you are not pleased!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGordon fixed his eyes on the ground a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI congratulate you on your engagement,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last, looking up\r\nwith a face that seemed to Bernard hard and unnatural.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very good of you to say that, but of course you can\xe2\x80\x99t like it! I\r\nwas sure you would n\xe2\x80\x99t like it. But what could I do? I fell in love with\r\nher, and I could n\xe2\x80\x99t run away simply to spare you a surprise.
