My dear\r\nGordon,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard added, \xe2\x80\x9cyou will get used to it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery likely,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gordon, dryly. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you must give me time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs long as you like!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGordon stood for a moment again staring down at the ground.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, then, I will take my time,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd he turned away, as if to walk off alone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere are you going?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Bernard, stopping him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know--to the hotel, anywhere. To try to get used to what you\r\nhave told me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t try too hard; it will come of itself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall see!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Gordon turned away again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you prefer to go alone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery much--if you will excuse me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have asked you to excuse a greater want of ceremony!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard,\r\nsmiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not done so yet!\xe2\x80\x9d Gordon rejoined; and marching off, he mingled\r\nwith the crowd.\r\n\r\nBernard watched him till he lost sight of him, and then, dropping into\r\nthe first empty chair that he saw, he sat and reflected that his friend\r\nliked it quite as little as he had feared.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVI\r\n\r\nBernard sat thinking for a long time; at first with a good deal of\r\nmortification--at last with a good deal of bitterness. He felt angry\r\nat last; but he was not angry with himself. He was displeased with poor\r\nGordon, and with Gordon\xe2\x80\x99s displeasure. He was uncomfortable, and he was\r\nvexed at his discomfort. It formed, it seemed to him, no natural part of\r\nhis situation; he had had no glimpse of it in the book of fate where he\r\nregistered on a fair blank page his betrothal to a charming girl. That\r\nGordon should be surprised, and even a little shocked and annoyed--this\r\nwas his right and his privilege; Bernard had been prepared for that, and\r\nhad determined to make the best of it. But it must not go too far; there\r\nwere limits to the morsel of humble pie that he was disposed to swallow.\r\nSomething in Gordon\xe2\x80\x99s air and figure, as he went off in a huff, looking\r\nvicious and dangerous--yes, that was positively his look--left a\r\nsinister impression on Bernard\xe2\x80\x99s mind, and, after a while, made him\r\nglad to take refuge in being angry. One would like to know what Gordon\r\nexpected, par exemple! Did he expect Bernard to give up Angela simply\r\nto save him a shock; or to back out of his engagement by way of an ideal\r\nreparation? No, it was too absurd, and, if Gordon had a wife of his own,\r\nwhy in the name of justice should not Bernard have one?\r\n\r\nBeing angry was a relief, but it was not exactly a solution, and\r\nBernard, at last, leaving his place, where for an hour or two he had\r\nbeen absolutely unconscious of everything that went on around him,\r\nwandered about for some time in deep restlessness and irritation. At\r\none moment he thought of going back to Gordon\xe2\x80\x99s hotel, to see him, to\r\nexplain. But then he became aware that he was too angry for that--to say\r\nnothing of Gordon\xe2\x80\x99s being too angry also; and, moreover, that there\r\nwas nothing to explain. He was to marry Angela Vivian; that was a\r\nvery simple fact--it needed no explanation. Was it so wonderful, so\r\ninconceivable, an incident so unlikely to happen? He went, as he always\r\ndid on Sunday, to dine with Mrs. Vivian, and it seemed to him that he\r\nperceived in the two ladies some symptoms of a discomposure which had\r\nthe same origin as his own. Bernard, on this occasion, at dinner, failed\r\nto make himself particularly agreeable; he ate fast--as if he had no\r\nidea what he was eating, and talked little; every now and then his\r\neyes rested for some time upon Angela, with a strange, eagerly excited\r\nexpression, as if he were looking her over and trying to make up his\r\nmind about her afresh. This young lady bore his inscrutable scrutiny\r\nwith a deal of superficial composure; but she was also silent, and she\r\nreturned his gaze, from time to time, with an air of unusual anxiety.\r\nShe was thinking, of course, of Gordon, Bernard said to himself; and a\r\nwoman\xe2\x80\x99s first meeting, in after years, with an ex-lover must always make\r\na certain impression upon her. Gordon, however, had never been a\r\nlover, and if Bernard noted Angela\xe2\x80\x99s gravity it was not because he felt\r\njealous. \xe2\x80\x9cShe is simply sorry for him,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself; and by the\r\ntime he had finished his dinner it began to come back to him that he\r\nwas sorry, too. Mrs. Vivian was probably sorry as well, for she had a\r\nslightly confused and preoccupied look--a look from which, even in the\r\nmidst of his chagrin, Bernard extracted some entertainment. It was Mrs.\r\nVivian\xe2\x80\x99s intermittent conscience that had been reminded of one of its\r\nlapses; her meeting with Gordon Wright had recalled the least exemplary\r\nepisode of her life--the time when she whispered mercenary counsel in\r\nthe ear of a daughter who sat, grave and pale, looking at her with eyes\r\nthat wondered. Mrs. Vivian blushed a little now, when she met Bernard\xe2\x80\x99s\r\neyes; and to remind herself that she was after all a virtuous woman,\r\ntalked as much as possible about superior and harmless things--the\r\nbeauty of the autumn weather, the pleasure of seeing French papas\r\nwalking about on Sunday with their progeny in their hands, the\r\npeculiarities of the pulpit-oratory of the country as exemplified in\r\nthe discourse of a Protestant pasteur whom she had been to hear in the\r\nmorning.\r\n\r\nWhen they rose from table and went back into her little drawing-room,\r\nshe left her daughter alone for awhile with Bernard. The two were\r\nstanding together before the fire; Bernard watched Mrs. Vivian close the\r\ndoor softly behind her. Then, looking for a moment at his companion--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is furious!\xe2\x80\x9d he announced at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFurious?\xe2\x80\x9d said Angela. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean Mr. Wright?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe amiable, reasonable Gordon. He takes it very hard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean about me?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Angela.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt \xe2\x80\x98s not with you he \xe2\x80\x98s furious, of course; it is with me. He won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlet me off easily.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAngela looked for a moment at the fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very sorry for him,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to me I am the one to be pitied,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard; \xe2\x80\x9cand I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsee what compassion you, of all people in the world, owe him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAngela again rested her eyes on the fire; then presently, looking up--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe liked me very much,\xe2\x80\x9d she remarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll the more shame to him!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bernard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the girl, with her beautiful stare.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf he liked you, why did he give you up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe did n\xe2\x80\x99t give me up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean, please?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Bernard, staring back at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI sent him away--I refused him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Angela.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; but you thought better of it, and your mother had persuaded you\r\nthat if he should ask you again, you had better accept him. Then it was\r\nthat he backed out--in consequence of what I said to him on his return\r\nfrom England.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe shook her head slowly, with a strange smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy poor Bernard, you are talking very wildly. He did ask me again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat night?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bernard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe night he came back from England--the last time I saw him, until\r\nto-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter I had denounced you?\xe2\x80\x9d our puzzled hero exclaimed, frowning\r\nportentously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry to let you know the small effect of your words!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard folded his hands together--almost devoutly--and stood gazing at\r\nher with a long, inarticulate murmur of satisfaction.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! then, I did n\xe2\x80\x99t injure you--I did n\xe2\x80\x99t deprive you of a chance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, sir, the intention on your part was the same!\xe2\x80\x9d Angela exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen all my uneasiness, all my remorse, were wasted?\xe2\x80\x9d he went on.\r\n\r\nBut she kept the same tone, and its tender archness only gave a greater\r\nsweetness to his sense of relief.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was a very small penance for you to pay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou dismissed him definitely, and that was why he vanished?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\r\nBernard, wondering still.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe gave me another \xe2\x80\x98chance,\xe2\x80\x99 as you elegantly express it, and I\r\ndeclined to take advantage of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, well, now,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bernard, \xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry for him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was very kind--very respectful,\xe2\x80\x9d said Angela. \xe2\x80\x9cI thanked him from the\r\nbottom of my heart; I begged his pardon very humbly for the wrong--if\r\nwrong it was--that I was doing him. I did n\xe2\x80\x99t in the least require of\r\nhim that he should leave Baden at seven o\xe2\x80\x99clock the next morning. I had\r\nno idea that he would do so, and that was the reason that I insisted to\r\nmy mother that we ourselves should go away. When we went I knew nothing\r\nabout his having gone, and I supposed he was still there. I did n\xe2\x80\x99t wish\r\nto meet him again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAngela gave this information slowly, softly, with pauses between the\r\nsentences, as if she were recalling the circumstances with a certain\r\neffort; and meanwhile Bernard, with his transfigured face and his eyes\r\nfixed upon her lips, was moving excitedly about the room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, he can\xe2\x80\x99t accuse me, then!\xe2\x80\x9d he broke out again. \xe2\x80\x9cIf what I said\r\nhad no more effect upon him than that, I certainly did him no wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you are rather vexed he did n\xe2\x80\x99t believe you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Angela.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI confess I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand it. He had all the air of it. He certainly\r\nhad not the air of a man who was going to rush off and give you the last\r\nproof of his confidence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was not a proof of confidence,\xe2\x80\x9d said Angela. \xe2\x80\x9cIt had nothing to\r\ndo with me. It was as between himself and you; it was a proof of\r\nindependence. He did believe you, more or less, and what you said fell\r\nin with his own impressions--strange impressions that they were, poor\r\nman! At the same time, as I say, he liked me, too; it was out of his\r\nliking me that all his trouble came! He caught himself in the act of\r\nlistening to you too credulously--and that seemed to him unmanly and\r\ndishonorable. The sensation brought with it a reaction, and to prove\r\nto himself that in such a matter he could be influenced by nobody, he\r\nmarched away, an hour after he had talked with you, and, in the teeth\r\nof his perfect mistrust, confirmed by your account of my\r\nirregularities--heaven forgive you both!--again asked me to be his wife.\r\nBut he hoped I would refuse!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bernard, \xe2\x80\x9cthe recreant! He deserved--he deserved--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat I should accept him?\xe2\x80\x9d Angela asked, smiling still.\r\n\r\nBernard was so much affected by this revelation, it seemed to him to\r\nmake such a difference in his own responsibility and to lift such a\r\nweight off his conscience, that he broke out again into the liveliest\r\nejaculations of relief.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t care for anything, now, and I can do what I please! Gordon\r\nmay hate me, and I shall be sorry for him; but it \xe2\x80\x98s not my fault, and I\r\nowe him no reparation. No, no; I am free!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt \xe2\x80\x98s only I who am not, I suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d said Angela, \xe2\x80\x9cand the reparation\r\nmust come from me! If he is unhappy, I must take the responsibility.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh yes, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, kissing her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why should he be unhappy?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Angela. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I refused him, it was\r\nwhat he wanted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is hard to please,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard rejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cHe has got a wife of his\r\nown.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf Blanche does n\xe2\x80\x99t please him, he is certainly difficult;\xe2\x80\x9d and Angela\r\nmused a little. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you told me the other day that they were getting on\r\nso well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I believe I told you,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard answered, musing a little too.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not attending to what I say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I am thinking of something else--I am thinking of what it was that\r\nmade you refuse him that way, at the last, after you had let your mother\r\nhope.\xe2\x80\x9d And Bernard stood there, smiling at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t think any more; you will not find out,\xe2\x80\x9d the girl declared,\r\nturning away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, it was cruel of you to let me think I was wrong all these years,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n he went on; \xe2\x80\x9cand, at the time, since you meant to refuse him, you might\r\nhave been more frank with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought my fault had been that I was too frank.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was densely stupid, and you might have made me understand better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Angela, \xe2\x80\x9cyou ask a great deal of a girl!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy have you let me go on so long thinking that my deluded words had\r\nhad an effect upon Gordon--feeling that I had done you a brutal wrong?\r\nIt was real to me, the wrong--and I have told you of the pangs and the\r\nshame which, for so many months, it has cost me! Why have you never\r\nundeceived me until to-day, and then only by accident?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this question Angela blushed a little; then she answered, smiling--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was my vengeance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard shook his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat won\xe2\x80\x99t do--you don\xe2\x80\x99t mean it. You never cared--you were too proud\r\nto care; and when I spoke to you about my fault, you did n\xe2\x80\x99t even know\r\nwhat I meant. You might have told me, therefore, that my remorse\r\nwas idle, that what I said to Gordon had not been of the smallest\r\nconsequence, and that the rupture had come from yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor some time Angela said nothing, then at last she gave him one of the\r\ndeeply serious looks with which her face was occasionally ornamented.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you want really to know, then--can\xe2\x80\x99t you see that your remorse\r\nseemed to me connected in a certain way with your affection; a sort of\r\nguarantee of it? You thought you had injured some one or other, and\r\nthat seemed to be mixed up with your loving me, and therefore I let it\r\nalone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, \xe2\x80\x9cmy remorse is all gone, and yet I think I love you\r\nabout as much as ever! So you see how wrong you were not to tell me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe wrong to you I don\xe2\x80\x99t care about. It is very true I might have told\r\nyou for Mr. Wright\xe2\x80\x99s sake. It would perhaps have made him look better.\r\nBut as you never attacked him for deserting me, it seemed needless for\r\nme to defend him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI confess,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, \xe2\x80\x9cI am quite at sea about Gordon\xe2\x80\x99s look in\r\nthe matter. Is he looking better now--or is he looking worse? You put it\r\nvery well just now; I was attending to you, though you said I was not.\r\nIf he hoped you would refuse him, with whom is his quarrel at present?\r\nAnd why was he so cool to me for months after we parted at Baden? If\r\nthat was his state of mind, why should he accuse me of inconsistency?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is something in it, after all, that a woman can understand. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know whether a man can. He hoped I would refuse him, and yet when\r\nI had done so he was vexed. After a while his vexation subsided, and he\r\nmarried poor Blanche; but, on learning to-day that I had accepted you,\r\nit flickered up again. He has such an expressive face.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe looked at me very kindly, mamma,\xe2\x80\x9d Angela observed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe regularly stared, my daughter. In any one else I should have said\r\nit was rude. But his situation is so peculiar; and one could see that he\r\nadmired you still.\xe2\x80\x9d And Mrs. Vivian gave a little soft sigh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! she is thinking of the thirty thousand a year,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard said to\r\nhimself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure I hope he admires me still,\xe2\x80\x9d the girl cried, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\r\nis no great harm in that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was comparing you with Blanche--and he was struck with the\r\ncontrast.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt could n\xe2\x80\x99t have been in my favor. I\r\nam so glad she is to be near us again. I think a great deal can be done\r\nby association. We must help her, Angela. I think we helped her before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is also very true that she is light, Mrs. Vivian,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard observed,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand if you could make her a little heavier, I should be tremendously\r\ngrateful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard\xe2\x80\x99s prospective mother-in-law looked at him a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know whether you are laughing at me--I always think you are.\r\nBut I shall not give up Blanche for that. I never give up any one that I\r\nhave once tried to help. Blanche will come back to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Vivian had hardly spoken when the sharp little vibration of her\r\ndoor-bell was heard in the hall. Bernard stood for a moment looking at\r\nthe door of the drawing-room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is poor Gordon come to make a scene!\xe2\x80\x9d he announced.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that what you mean--that he opposed your marriage?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mrs.\r\nVivian, with a frightened air.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what he proposes to do with Blanche,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard,\r\nlaughing.\r\n\r\nThere were voices in the hall. Angela had been listening.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou say she will come back to you, mamma,\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cHere she is\r\narrived!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVII\r\n\r\nAt the same moment the door was thrown open, and Mrs. Gordon appeared\r\non the threshold with a gentleman behind her. Blanche stood an instant\r\nlooking into the lighted room and hesitating--flushed a little, smiling,\r\nextremely pretty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay I come in?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cand may I bring in Captain Lovelock?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two ladies, of course, fluttering toward her with every\r\ndemonstration of hospitality, drew her into the room, while Bernard\r\nproceeded to greet the Captain, who advanced with a certain awkward and\r\nbashful majesty, almost sweeping with his great stature Mrs. Vivian\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhumble ceiling. There was a tender exchange of embraces between Blanche\r\nand her friends, and the charming visitor, losing no time, began to\r\nchatter with her usual volubility. Mrs. Vivian and Angela made her\r\ncompanion graciously welcome; but Blanche begged they would n\xe2\x80\x99t mind\r\nhim--she had only brought him as a watch-dog.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis place is on the rug,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cCaptain Lovelock, go and lie down\r\non the rug.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon my soul, there is nothing else but rugs in these French places!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n the Captain rejoined, looking round Mrs. Vivian\xe2\x80\x99s salon. \xe2\x80\x9cWhich rug do\r\nyou mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Vivian had remarked to Blanche that it was very kind of her to come\r\nfirst, and Blanche declared that she could not have laid her head on her\r\npillow before she had seen her dear Mrs. Vivian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you suppose I would wait because I am married?\xe2\x80\x9d she inquired, with\r\na keen little smile in her charming eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cI am not so much married as\r\nthat, I can tell you! Do you think I look much as if I were married,\r\nwith no one to bring me here to-night but Captain Lovelock?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure Captain Lovelock is a very gallant escort,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Vivian.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, he was not afraid--that is, he was not afraid of the journey,\r\nthough it lay all through those dreadful wild Champs Elysees. But when\r\nwe arrived, he was afraid to come in--to come up here. Captain Lovelock\r\nis so modest, you know--in spite of all the success he had in America.\r\nHe will tell you about the success he had in America; it quite makes up\r\nfor the defeat of the British army in the Revolution. They were defeated\r\nin the Revolution, the British, were n\xe2\x80\x99t they? I always told him so, but\r\nhe insists they were not. \xe2\x80\x98How do we come to be free, then?\xe2\x80\x99 I always\r\nask him; \xe2\x80\x98I suppose you admit that we are free.\xe2\x80\x99 Then he becomes\r\npersonal and says that I am free enough, certainly. I\r\nthink he would believe you, because he knows you know a great deal about\r\nhistory and all that. I don\xe2\x80\x99t mean this evening, but some time when\r\nit is convenient. He did n\xe2\x80\x99t want to come in--he wanted to stay in the\r\ncarriage and smoke a cigar; he thought you would n\xe2\x80\x99t like it, his coming\r\nwith me the first time. But I told him he need n\xe2\x80\x99t mind that, for I\r\nwould certainly explain. I would be very careful to let you know that\r\nI brought him only as a substitute. A substitute for whom? A substitute\r\nfor my husband, of course. My dear Mrs. Vivian, of course I ought to\r\nbring you some pretty message from Gordon--that he is dying to come and\r\nsee you, only that he had nineteen letters to write and that he could\r\nn\xe2\x80\x99t possibly stir from his fireside. I suppose a good wife ought to\r\ninvent excuses for her husband--ought to throw herself into the breach;\r\nis n\xe2\x80\x99t that what they call it? But I am afraid I am not a good wife.\r\nDo you think I am a good wife, Mr. Longueville? You once stayed three\r\nmonths with us, and you had a chance to see. I don\xe2\x80\x99t ask you that\r\nseriously, because you never tell the truth. I always do; so I will\r\nsay I am not a good wife. And then the breach is too big, and I am too\r\nlittle. Oh, I am too little, Mrs. Vivian; I know I am too little. I am\r\nthe smallest woman living; Gordon can scarcely see me with a microscope,\r\nand I believe he has the most powerful one in America. He is going to\r\nget another here; that is one of the things he came abroad for; perhaps\r\nit will do better. I do tell the truth, don\xe2\x80\x99t I, Mrs. Vivian? I have\r\nthat merit, if I have n\xe2\x80\x99t any other. You once told me so at Baden; you\r\nsaid you could say one thing for me, at any rate--that I did n\xe2\x80\x99t tell\r\nfibs. He simply declined,\r\nand he went out somewhere else. So you see he is n\xe2\x80\x99t writing letters. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know where he can have gone; perhaps he has gone to the theatre.\r\nI know it is n\xe2\x80\x99t proper to go to the theatre on Sunday evening; but\r\nthey say charity begins at home, and as Gordon\xe2\x80\x99s does n\xe2\x80\x99t begin at home,\r\nperhaps it does n\xe2\x80\x99t begin anywhere. I told him that if he would n\xe2\x80\x99t come\r\nwith me I would come alone, and he said I might do as I chose--that he\r\nwas not in a humor for making visits. I wanted to come to you very much;\r\nI had been thinking about it all day; and I am so fond of a visit like\r\nthis in the evening, without being invited. By good\r\nfortune he happened to have been dining with us; so I told him he must\r\nbring me here. I told you I would explain, Captain Lovelock,\xe2\x80\x9d she added,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand I hope you think I have made it clear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Captain had turned very red during this wandering discourse. He sat\r\npulling his beard and shifting the position which, with his stalwart\r\nperson, he had taken up on a little gilded chair--a piece of furniture\r\nwhich every now and then gave a delicate creak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI always understand you well enough till you begin to explain,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nrejoined, with a candid, even if embarrassed, laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cThen, by Jove,\r\nI \xe2\x80\x98m quite in the woods. Does n\xe2\x80\x99t she, Miss Vivian?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlanche has a fine imagination,\xe2\x80\x9d said Angela, smiling frankly at the\r\ncharming visitor.\r\n\r\nWhen Blanche was fairly adrift upon the current of her articulate\r\nreflections, it was the habit of her companions--indeed, it was a sort\r\nof tacit agreement among them--simply to make a circle and admire. They\r\nsat about and looked at her--yawning, perhaps, a little at times, but\r\non the whole very well entertained, and often exchanging a smiling\r\ncommentary with each other. She looked at them, smiled at them each,\r\nin succession. Every one had his turn, and this always helped to give\r\nBlanche an audience. Incoherent and aimless as much of her talk was, she\r\nnever looked prettier than in the attitude of improvisation--or rather,\r\nI should say, than in the hundred attitudes which she assumed at such a\r\ntime. Perpetually moving, she was yet constantly graceful, and while\r\nshe twisted her body and turned her head, with charming hands that never\r\nceased to gesticulate, and little, conscious, brilliant eyes that looked\r\neverywhere at once--eyes that seemed to chatter even faster than her\r\nlips--she made you forget the nonsense she poured forth, or think of it\r\nonly as a part of her personal picturesqueness. The thing was a regular\r\nperformance; the practice of unlimited chatter had made her perfect. She\r\nrested upon her audience and held it together, and the sight of half\r\na dozen pairs of amused and fascinated faces led her from one piece of\r\nfolly to another. On this occasion, her audience was far from failing\r\nher, for they were all greatly interested. Captain Lovelock\xe2\x80\x99s interest,\r\nas we know, was chronic, and our three other friends were much occupied\r\nwith a matter with which Blanche was intimately connected. Bernard,\r\nas he listened to her, smiling mechanically, was not encouraged. He\r\nremembered what Mrs. Vivian had said shortly before she came in, and it\r\nwas not pleasant to him to think that Gordon had been occupied half\r\nthe day in contrasting the finest girl in the world with this magnified\r\nbutterfly. The contrast was sufficiently striking as Angela sat there\r\nnear her, very still, bending her handsome head a little, with her hands\r\ncrossed in her lap, and on her lips a kind but inscrutable smile. Mrs.\r\nVivian was on the sofa next to Blanche, one of whose hands, when it was\r\nnot otherwise occupied, she occasionally took into her own.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear little Blanche!\xe2\x80\x9d she softly murmured, at intervals.\r\n\r\nThese few remarks represent a longer pause than Mrs. Gordon often\r\nsuffered to occur. She continued to deliver herself upon a hundred\r\ntopics, and it hardly matters where we take her up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have n\xe2\x80\x99t the least idea what we are going to do. I have nothing to\r\nsay about it whatever. Gordon tells me every day I must decide, and\r\nthen I ask Captain Lovelock what he thinks; because, you see, he always\r\nthinks a great deal. Captain Lovelock says he does n\xe2\x80\x99t care a fig--that\r\nhe will go wherever I go. So you see that does n\xe2\x80\x99t carry us very far.\r\nI want to settle on some place where Captain Lovelock won\xe2\x80\x99t go, but he\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t help me at all. I think it will look better for him not to follow\r\nus; don\xe2\x80\x99t you think it will look better, Mrs. Vivian? Gordon may go anywhere he likes--to St. Petersburg, or to\r\nBombay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou might go to a worse place than Bombay,\xe2\x80\x9d said Captain Lovelock,\r\nspeaking with the authority of an Anglo-Indian rich in reminiscences.\r\n\r\nBlanche gave him a little stare.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh well, that \xe2\x80\x98s knocked on the head! But we have got to go to some southern place,\r\nbecause I am very unwell. I have n\xe2\x80\x99t the least idea what \xe2\x80\x98s the matter\r\nwith me, and neither has any one else; but that does n\xe2\x80\x99t make any\r\ndifference. It \xe2\x80\x98s settled that I am out of health. One might as well\r\nbe out of it as in it, for all the advantage it is. If you are out of\r\nhealth, at any rate you can come abroad. It was Gordon\xe2\x80\x99s discovery--he\r\n\xe2\x80\x98s always making discoveries. You see it \xe2\x80\x98s because I \xe2\x80\x98m so silly; he\r\ncan always put it down to my being an invalid. What I should like to do,\r\nMrs. Vivian, would be to spend the winter with you--just sitting on the\r\nsofa beside you and holding your hand. It would be rather tiresome for\r\nyou; but I really think it would be better for me than anything else.\r\nI have never forgotten how kind you were to me before my marriage--that\r\nsummer at Baden. You were everything to me--you and Captain Lovelock. I\r\nam sure I should be happy if I never went out of this lovely room. You\r\nhave got it so beautifully arranged--I mean to do my own room just like\r\nit when I go home. And you have got such lovely clothes. You never used\r\nto say anything about it, but you and Angela always had better clothes\r\nthan I. Are you always so quiet and serious--never talking about\r\nchiffons--always reading some wonderful book? I wish you would let\r\nme come and stay with you. If you only ask me, Gordon would be too\r\ndelighted. He would n\xe2\x80\x99t have to trouble about me any more. He could go\r\nand live over in the Latin Quarter--that \xe2\x80\x98s the desire of his heart--and\r\nthink of nothing but old bottles. I know it is n\xe2\x80\x99t very good manners\r\nto beg for an invitation,\xe2\x80\x9d Blanche went on, smiling with a gentler\r\nradiance; \xe2\x80\x9cbut when it \xe2\x80\x98s a question of one\xe2\x80\x99s health. One wants to keep\r\none\xe2\x80\x99s self alive--does n\xe2\x80\x99t one? One wants to keep one\xe2\x80\x99s self going. It\r\nwould be so good for me, Mrs. Vivian; it would really be very good for\r\nme!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had turned round more and more to her hostess as she talked; and at\r\nlast she had given both her hands to Mrs. Vivian, and sat looking at her\r\nwith a singular mixture of earnestness and jocosity. It was hard to know\r\nwhether Blanche were expressing a real desire or a momentary caprice,\r\nand whether this abrupt little petition were to be taken seriously, or\r\ntreated merely as a dramatic pose in a series of more or less effective\r\nattitudes. Her smile had become almost a grimace, she was flushed, she\r\nshowed her pretty teeth; but there was a little passionate quiver in her\r\nvoice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear child,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Vivian, \xe2\x80\x9cwe should be delighted to have you\r\npay us a visit, and we should be so happy if we could do you any good.\r\nBut I am afraid you would very soon get tired of us, and I ought to tell\r\nyou, frankly, that our little home is to be--a broken up. After a moment, while Bernard stood there with his back\r\nturned--he felt rather awkward and foolish--he heard Blanche begin with\r\nher little surprised voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you are going away? You are going to travel? But that \xe2\x80\x98s charming;\r\nwe can travel together. You are not going to travel? What then are you\r\ngoing to do? You are going back to America? Ah, but you must n\xe2\x80\x99t do\r\nthat, as soon as I come abroad; that \xe2\x80\x98s not nice or friendly, Mrs.\r\nVivian, to your poor little old Blanche. You are not going back to\r\nAmerica? Ah, then, I give it up! What \xe2\x80\x98s the great mystery? Is it\r\nsomething about Angela? There was always a mystery about Angela. I hope\r\nyou won\xe2\x80\x99t mind my saying it, my dear; but I was always afraid of you.\r\nMy husband--he admires you so much, you know--has often tried to explain\r\nyou to me; but I have never understood. What are you going to do now?\r\nAre you going into a convent? Are you going to be--A-a-h!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd, suddenly, quickly, interrupting herself, Mrs. Gordon gave a long,\r\nwondering cry. Bernard heard her spring to her feet, and the two other\r\nladies rise from their seats. Captain Lovelock got up as well; Bernard\r\nheard him knock over his little gilded chair. There was a pause, during\r\nwhich Blanche went through a little mute exhibition of amazement and\r\npleasure. Bernard turned round, to receive half a dozen quick questions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you hiding away for? What are you blushing for? I never saw\r\nyou do anything like that before! Why do you look so strange, and what\r\nare you making me say? Angela, is it true--is there something like\r\nthat?\xe2\x80\x9d Without waiting for the answer to this last question, Blanche\r\nthrew herself upon Mrs. Vivian. \xe2\x80\x9cMy own Mrs. Vivian,\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, \xe2\x80\x9cis she\r\nmarried?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Blanche,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard, coming forward, \xe2\x80\x9chas not Gordon told\r\nyou? Angela and I are not married, but we hope to be before long. Gordon\r\nonly knew it this morning; we ourselves have only known it a short time.\r\nThere is no mystery about it, and we only want your congratulations.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I must say you have been very quiet about it!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Blanche.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I was engaged, I wrote you all a letter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy Jove, she wrote to me!\xe2\x80\x9d observed Captain Lovelock.\r\n\r\nAngela went to her and kissed her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour husband does n\xe2\x80\x99t seem to have explained me very successfully!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Gordon held Bernard\xe2\x80\x99s intended for a moment at arm\xe2\x80\x99s length, with\r\nboth her hands, looking at her with eyes of real excitement and wonder.\r\nThen she folded her in a prolonged, an exaggerated, embrace.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did n\xe2\x80\x99t he tell me--why did n\xe2\x80\x99t he tell me?\xe2\x80\x9d she presently began.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has had all day to tell me, and it was very cruel of him to let\r\nme come here without knowing it. Could anything be more absurd--more\r\nawkward? You don\xe2\x80\x99t think it \xe2\x80\x98s awkward--you don\xe2\x80\x99t mind it? Ah well, you\r\nare very good! But I like it, Angela--I like it extremely, immensely.\r\nI think it \xe2\x80\x98s delightful, and I wonder it never occurred to me. Has\r\nit been going on long? Ah, of course, it has been going on! Did n\xe2\x80\x99t it\r\nbegin at Baden, and did n\xe2\x80\x99t I see it there? Do you mind my alluding to\r\nthat? At Baden we were all so mixed up that one could n\xe2\x80\x99t tell who was\r\nattentive to whom! But Bernard has been very faithful, my dear; I can\r\nassure you of that. When he was in America he would n\xe2\x80\x99t look at another\r\nwoman. I know something about that! He stayed three months in my house\r\nand he never spoke to me. Now I know why, Mr. Bernard; but you might\r\nhave told me at the time. The reason was certainly good enough. I always\r\nwant to know why, you know. Why Gordon never told me, for instance; that\r\n\xe2\x80\x98s what I want to know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBlanche refused to sit down again; she declared that she was so agitated\r\nby this charming news that she could not be quiet, and that she must\r\npresently take her departure. Meanwhile she congratulated each of her\r\nfriends half a dozen times; she kissed Mrs. Vivian again, she almost\r\nkissed Bernard; she inquired about details; she longed to hear all\r\nabout Angela\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cthings.\xe2\x80\x9d Of course they would stop for the wedding;\r\nbut meantime she must be very discreet; she must not intrude too\r\nmuch. Captain Lovelock addressed to Angela a few fragmentary, but\r\nwell-intentioned sentences, pulling his beard and fixing his eyes on the\r\ndoor-knob--an implement which presently turned in his manly fist, as he\r\nopened the door for his companion to withdraw. Blanche went away in a\r\nflutter of ejaculations and protestations which left our three friends\r\nin Mrs. Vivian\xe2\x80\x99s little drawing-room standing looking at each other as\r\nthe door closed behind her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt certainly would have been better taste in him to tell her,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Bernard, frowning, \xe2\x80\x9cand not let other people see how little\r\ncommunication there is between them. It has mortified her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor Mr. Wright had his reasons,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Vivian suggested, and then she\r\nventured to explain: \xe2\x80\x9cHe still cares for Angela, and it was painful to\r\nhim to talk about her marrying some one else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis had been Bernard\xe2\x80\x99s own reflection, and it was no more agreeable as\r\nMrs. Vivian presented it; though Angela herself seemed indifferent to\r\nit--seemed, indeed, not to hear it, as if she were thinking of something\r\nelse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must simply marry as soon as possible; to-morrow, if necessary,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Bernard, with some causticity. \xe2\x80\x9cThat \xe2\x80\x98s the best thing we can do\r\nfor every one. When once Angela is married, Gordon will stop thinking\r\nof her. He will never permit his imagination to hover about a married\r\nwoman; I am very sure of that. He does n\xe2\x80\x99t approve of that sort of\r\nthing, and he has the same law for himself as for other people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt does n\xe2\x80\x99t matter,\xe2\x80\x9d said Angela, simply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you mean, my daughter, it does n\xe2\x80\x99t matter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t feel obliged to feel so sorry for him now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow? Pray, what has happened? I am more sorry than ever, since I have\r\nheard poor Blanche\xe2\x80\x99s dreadful tone about him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe girl was silent a moment; then she shook her head, lightly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHer tone--her tone? Dearest mother, don\xe2\x80\x99t you see? She is intensely in\r\nlove with him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVIII\r\n\r\nThis observation struck Bernard as extremely ingenious and worthy of his\r\nmistress\xe2\x80\x99s fine intelligence; he greeted it with enthusiasm, and thought\r\nof it for the next twelve hours. The more he thought of it the more\r\nfelicitous it seemed to him, and he went to Mrs. Vivian\xe2\x80\x99s the next day\r\nalmost for the express purpose of saying to Angela that, decidedly,\r\nshe was right. He was admitted by his old friend, the little femme\r\nde chambre, who had long since bestowed upon him, definitively, her\r\nconfidence; and as in the ante-chamber he heard the voice of a gentleman\r\nraised and talking with some emphasis, come to him from the salon, he\r\npaused a moment, looking at her with an interrogative eye.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Vivian\xe2\x80\x99s attendant, \xe2\x80\x9cI must tell Monsieur frankly that\r\nanother gentleman is there. Moreover, what does it matter? Monsieur\r\nwould perceive it for himself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas he been here long?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Bernard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA quarter of an hour. It probably does n\xe2\x80\x99t seem long to the gentleman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs he alone with Mademoiselle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe asked for Mademoiselle only. I introduced him into the salon, and\r\nMademoiselle, after conversing a little while with Madame, consented\r\nto receive him. They have been alone together, as I have told Monsieur,\r\nsince about three o\xe2\x80\x99clock. Madame is in her own apartment. The position\r\nof Monsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d added this discriminating woman, \xe2\x80\x9ccertainly justifies him\r\nin entering the salon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBernard was quite of this opinion, and in a moment more he had crossed\r\nthe threshold of the little drawing-room and closed the door behind him.\r\n\r\nAngela sat there on a sofa, leaning back with her hands clasped in her\r\nlap and her eyes fixed upon Gordon Wright, who stood squarely before\r\nher, as if he had been making her a resolute speech. Her face wore a\r\nlook of distress, almost of alarm; she kept her place, but her eyes gave\r\nBernard a mute welcome. Gordon turned and looked at him slowly from head\r\nto foot. Bernard remembered, with a good deal of vividness, the last\r\nlook his friend had given him in the Champs Elysees the day before; and\r\nhe saw with some satisfaction that this was not exactly a repetition of\r\nthat expression of cold horror. It was a question, however, whether the\r\nhorror were changed for the better. Poor Gordon looked intensely sad and\r\ngrievously wronged. The keen resentment had faded from his face, but\r\nan immense reproach was there--a heavy, helpless, appealing reproach.\r\nBernard saw that he had not a scene of violence to dread--and yet, when\r\nhe perceived what was coming, he would almost have preferred violence.\r\nGordon did not offer him his hand, and before Bernard had had time to\r\nsay anything, began to speak again, as if he were going on with what he\r\nhad been saying to Angela.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have done me a great wrong--you have done me a cruel wrong! I have\r\nbeen telling it to Miss Vivian; I came on purpose to tell her. I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nreally tell her; I can\xe2\x80\x99t tell her the details; it \xe2\x80\x98s too painful! But\r\nyou know what I mean! I could n\xe2\x80\x99t stand it any longer. I thought of\r\ngoing away--but I could n\xe2\x80\x99t do that. I must come and say what I feel. I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t bear it now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis outbreak of a passionate sense of injury in a man habitually so\r\nundemonstrative, so little disposed to call attention to himself, had in\r\nit something at once of the touching and the terrible. Bernard, for an\r\ninstant, felt almost bewildered; he asked himself whether he had not,\r\nafter all, been a monster of duplicity. He was guilty of the weakness\r\nof taking refuge in what is called, I believe, in legal phrase, a\r\nside-issue.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say all this before Angela!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed, with a kind of\r\nartificial energy. \xe2\x80\x9cYou know she is not in the least at fault, and that\r\nit can only give her pain. The thing is between ourselves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAngela was sitting there, looking up at both the men. \xe2\x80\x9cI like to hear\r\nit,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have a singular taste!\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard declared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it \xe2\x80\x98s between ourselves,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Gordon, \xe2\x80\x9cand that Miss Vivian is\r\nnot at fault. She is only too lovely, too wise, too good! It is you\r\nand I that are at fault--horribly at fault! You see I admit it, and you\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t. I never dreamed that I should live to say such things as this\r\nto you; but I never dreamed you would do what you have done! It \xe2\x80\x98s\r\nhorrible, most horrible, that such a difference as this should come\r\nbetween two men who believed themselves--or whom I believed, at\r\nleast--the best friends in the world. For it is a difference--it \xe2\x80\x98s a\r\ngreat gulf, and nothing will ever fill it up. I must say so; I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhelp it. You know I don\xe2\x80\x99t express myself easily; so, if I break out this\r\nway, you may know what I feel. I know it is a pain to Miss Vivian, and\r\nI beg her to forgive me. She has so much to forgive that she can forgive\r\nthat, too. I can\xe2\x80\x99t pretend to accept it; I can\xe2\x80\x99t sit down and let it\r\npass. And then, it is n\xe2\x80\x99t only my feelings; it \xe2\x80\x98s the right; it \xe2\x80\x98s the\r\njustice. I must say to her that you have no right to marry her; and beg\r\nof her to listen to me and let you go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Gordon, are you crazy?\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard demanded, with an energy which,\r\nthis time at least, was sufficiently real.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery likely I am crazy. Add to that the wretchedness of what I\r\nhave found!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, don\xe2\x80\x99t say that, Mr. Wright,\xe2\x80\x9d Angela begged.\r\n\r\nHe stood for an instant looking at her, but not heeding her words.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you listen to me again? In listening to him, in\r\nbelieving him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you did n\xe2\x80\x99t believe me,\xe2\x80\x9d Bernard exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9csince you immediately\r\nwent and offered yourself to Miss Vivian!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believed you all the same! When did I ever not believe you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe last words I ever heard from Mr. Wright were words of the deepest\r\nkindness,\xe2\x80\x9d said Angela.\r\n\r\nShe spoke with such a serious, tender grace, that Gordon seemed stirred\r\nto his depths again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, give me another chance!\xe2\x80\x9d he moaned.\r\n\r\nThe poor girl could not help her tone, and it was in the same tone that\r\nshe continued--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you think so well of me, try and be reasonable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGordon looked at her, slowly shaking his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReasonable--reasonable? What I ask is within reasonable limits.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGranting your happiness were lost,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bernard--\xe2\x80\x9cI say that only for\r\nthe argument--is that a ground for your wishing to deprive me of mine?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not yours--it is mine, that you have taken! Yours is elsewhere, and you are welcome to\r\nit!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Bernard, giving him a long look and turning away, \xe2\x80\x9cit is\r\nwell for you that I am willing still to regard you as my best friend!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGordon went on, more passionately, to Angela.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe put me off my guard--I can\xe2\x80\x99t call it anything else. Your word had such weight with me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow many times more would you have wished to be refused, and how many\r\nrefusals would have been required to give me my liberty?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Bernard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat question means nothing, because you never knew that I had again\r\noffered myself to Miss Vivian.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; you told me very little, considering all that you made me tell\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told you beforehand that I should do exactly as I chose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should have allowed me the same liberty!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLiberty!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Gordon. \xe2\x80\x9cHad n\xe2\x80\x99t you liberty to range the whole world\r\nover? At\r\nthis fact Gordon stood staring; then at last he apprehended it--largely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, then, it had been a plot between you!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried out.\r\n\r\nBernard and Angela exchanged a glance of pity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe had met for five minutes, and had exchanged a few words before I\r\ncame to Baden. Bernard followed her for\r\nthree seconds with his eyes; then he went on--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it were so, I had no reason to suppose it. You have accused me of\r\ndeceiving you, but I deceived only myself. You say I put you off your\r\nguard, but you should rather say you put me on mine.
